Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To 11. F. IvyoKLMANx, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. / consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a areal
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting a wag' until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last 1 began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. 1 agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
PkTER S.MVTIIB, J.P.

DESCRIPTION.
The Platter or Bowl, marked
B, is the receptacle for holding
11b. of butter.
The Cover, marked A, is filled
with water at the top; as this
evaporates through the porous
cover, it produces a great degree
of cold, making the butter quite
HARD and FIRM during the
HOTTEST WEATHER.
OUR INSTRUCTIONS FOR
USE.
Soak the whole of the Butter
Cooler in clean water for twenty-
four hours. The butter can then
be placed in the dish marked B,
at the same time filling the hole
in the top with cold water.
The water must be replenished
from time to time as necessary,
but the whole cooler need only
be soaked once.

NOTICE.
By sending direct io The Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
Sterling—which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 8, 1890.
Exit Carington.
The retirement of Baron Cakington from
the Governorship of New South Wales, pro-
duced what the Sydney daily sup-Press tries
to put on record as a series of spontaneous
outbursts of esteem, loyalty and affection.
He was balled and banqueted, dinnered and
wined, speeched and sung to. Churches and
theatres, orphan-asylums and night refuges,
sporting clubs, literary clubs, and clubs with-
out either energy for sport, or desire for
literature, metaphorically prostrated them-
selves before him, and in their several ways
paid homage to him. His departure from
Government House was a pageant. Sixteen
guns saluted from the Inner Domain. The
carriage-way was strewn with flowers. The
carriage which bore himself and his wife
was a moving flower-stall. Brigades of
cyclists wheeled in front; cavalry, equipped as
lancers, played escort; volunteers, engineers,
artillery, naval forces, mounted infantry, staff
corps, cadet corps, and numerous other
branches enthusiastically assisted. Windows,
balconies, and streets were crammed with
miscellaneous throngs. Cheers and music
alternately drowned one another’s uproar,
and as the train carried oft' the ex-guberna-
torial pair, a clamour of exclamation filled
the air.
* * #
Now, in sober earnest, what is the signifi-
cance of it all ? Is it to be taken that
the sincerity of the homage was equal to
its volume ? Is it to be taken that if
even a small portion of the homage was
sincere that portion was gained by the lofty
exercise of any high power either of Lord
Caßinoton’s head or of his heart? As an
English “ nobleman,” representing Royalty
in Australia, he possessed a ready-made
entourage, but that entourage stands pre-
pared to transfer the best of all it gave to
him to any man, similarly accredited, who
may succeed him. With it go the men in
red and the men in brown, the men of the
flashing sabres and those of the gleaming
brasses, the sleek-faced functionaries and
powdered officials, the politicians who
climbed eminences for a parting view, and
the swarms of husbands and matrons who
frequent Government House with designs of
ulterior personal or family aggrandisement.
Some of these worship Royalty or its simu-
lacrum as a present means of living. They
must bear the- sabre, or blow the bugle, or
spur the horse, or start the cheers. They
are the servitors of existing authority, and
if the Crown were smashed up and the Throne
burnt to-morrow their enthusiastic service
would be purchaseable by the reddest-capped
incendiary who might mount to the place of
pay. Of the rest, almost all give worship, if not
also for pay one way or another, then because
of frailty. Their opera-bouffe conception
of human life has stripped manhood and
womanhood of natural grace and dignity
and in their places has put fantastic socia
tricks of segregation and isolation, for the
cultivation and perpetuation of which palaces
of gilded idleness and inept brain-work must
be maintained. Take away those who worship
for pay, present or prospective, and those
who worship because they mistake theatrical
social transformation-scenes for the splen-
dours of existence, and how numerous are
Caring ton’s special crowd of worshippers ?
# # #
He has, still, worshippers, it must be
granted. He played his Australian life to
that end and was not wholly a failure. With
a familiarity which in a free country partook
much of the character of an insolence he
entered into half the houses in the land.
No catastrophe was too appalling to suggest
to him reserve. The widow whose husband
lay stark before her had her hand seized by
the peripatetic intruder ; the husband who
tried to cover his face so that his tears should
not be witnessed by those standing around
the corp cs of wife and child, had a hand
pulled away from his eyes and wagged up
and down in gubernatorial condolence. If a
mine collapsed and entombed the workers the
Baron inflicted his sympathetic presence by
immediate despatch, and if a mattress caught
fire in the factory of a cheap drapery
store he was there beside the burn-
ing horsehair with an equally lugubrious
countenance and an equally fervent pressure
of the fat palm of the suffering soft-goods-
man. W hen flood-waters threatened an in-
land village he got there and squeezed the
hands of the homeless inhabitants and helped
things generally by pushing the steam-engine
with the tips of his Ungers, and when a foot-
baller was kicked in the stomach he was
there with his carriage to carry the heavy-
limbed youth to his obscure home. If a
baby was drowned in a tub of water he
electrically dispatched his sympathy to the
mother, if he could not make an immediate
raid on her himself ; and if a newspaper-
office took fire he corkscrewed himself
through the crowd and picking the tufty-
headed proprietor out played pump-handle
with him to the edification of standers-by
and the gratification of copy-seeking re-
porters, Indeed, newspapers were his choice
children. For those of them that lay down
flat and let Government House walk over
their columns he displayed a large and
generous appreciation. He gave their
editors to know that they were the equals
and in some respects the superiors of old-
world editors, and that if they could run
away any time from their desks and drop in
informally upon him his delight would be
unbounded. This was not amiability thrown
away. If he gave £5 to a starving
family the metropolitan papers emblazoned
the item, and it was echoed and re-
echoed and re-printed around the land
until it became £5OO, If he attended
a school-picnic or cricket-match, a sheaf of
notebooks were ready to receive his golden
commonplaces. However platitudinous his
remarks, the columns were never too full to
give him space. Practically, he was accepted
as a deadhead self-advertiser. Deduct those
influenced by this flagrant lord-worship of
the metropolitan Press, and the crowds that
lined the streets and filled balconies and
windows will be largely cut into, as they
would be when the Barnumesque tricks and
devices of any other self-advertising, would-
be hero are laid bare to public gaze.
# • #
After all these deductions, the balance is
small. But can it be said that even this
balance worshipped because of Carington’s
lofty qualities of head or heart ? In the
sphere of the latter the showman instinct
preponderated, as we have seen. It ex-
hibited the bizarre incongruities, ludicrous
inconsequentialities and unconscious anti-
climaxes into which a man, eternally
efforting to make a splash, must frequently
ignominiously fall. But in the sphere of
head—we defy any man in Australia to say
that a weaker-headed Governor ever ruled
over New South Wales. There stands to
his credit not one evidence of conspicuous
mental power, not one evidence of innate
insight into the needs of a oreat occasion,
not one symptom of touch with a law that
lasts longer than written law. And he had
his opportunity ! Burning like a brand of
shame upon the face of his administration is
the flagrant national outrage perpetrated by
him, in his capacity of Governor and
Queen’s representative, when the four
Mount Rennie youths—all under twenty
years of age—were swung from the gallows
in Darlinghurst Gaol. His utterly puerile
conception of the conditions under which the
power reposed in him should be exercised
robs the Royal prerogative of mercy for ever
of respect in these Southern (Hands. He, a
mere political stripling, a stranger to our
skies and shores, allowed the massacre of
these boys when the experience of the
country, the knowledge of the country, and
the sane virtue of the country implored that
the death-sentence should be commuted to
imprisonment. Cahington had not the
brains to see that the honour of the
country, the justice of the country, the
reputation of the Grown, were involved ;
his intellect only went the length of show-
ing him that the distinctly mandatory
words in which the Royal Prerogative was
robed admitted of the exercise of some
form of discretion, and quibbling through
these words he directed his discretion to
the “ popular ” course—a course made
popular by the lies and suppressions of an
institution upon which lie ever kept his eye.
Mr. Dallky, P.C., warned Gov. Carington
of the monstrous error which would be
committed if the Royal Prerogative were
not exercised. That eminent barrister
declared, not hurriedly, but after going
through, the case and all its surroundings
minutely from start to finish, that the execu-
tion of the youths would be a crime against
the Constitution, and that Parliament, in
authorising the Criminal Code under which
they were sentenced, never meant its pro-
visions to apply to such a case as that in
hand. He pointed out that only in the
darkest days of Irish history was a trial con-
ducted as Judge Wi.ndeyer had conducted
the trial of those youths. He contended
that counsel and jury should both have
refused to proceed with the trial in the cir-
cumstances in which they were placed.
* * *
This article we mean principally for
English readers, amongst whom Baron
Carington will no doubt now strive to
pose as an ideal administrator of Aus-
tralian affairs, and we, therefore, are com-
pelled tj repeat a few facts already known
to our other readers. Barrister O’Mara,
who defended one of the prisoners, wrote
directly to the Governor, pleading that for
his part he had been driven to exhaustion by
the manner in which the trial had been con-
ducted. As an example, the jurymen had
been kept in the box for (with five hours’
intermission) thirty-eight consecutive hours
on the two last days and nights of the
hearing—that is, from 9 a.m. on Friday
to 4 a.m. on Saturday, and again from
9 a.m. on Saturday till past 11 on Saturday
night ! The foreman of the jury admitted
that they hurried to a verdict as one of the
jurymen was breaking down ; and it was
also known that had they delayed longer
over their verdict they would have been
locked up till the next Monday. It is to
those incidents Dalley referred when he
spoke of the darkest hours of Irish history.
It was as a lawyer, as well as a statesman
and an adviser of the Queen, that he implored
the application of the Royal Prerogative.
But Carington, the man who knew nothing
about Australia or her legislation, but
held the Prerogative in his keeping, acted
as the weak man desiring a reputation for
strength uniformly acts, and instead of reason-
ing out his position clearly, snapped down on
his royal possession. Sir Henry Parke,s also
implored the exercise of the Royal Preroga-
tive. He declared that the execution of the
youths would be a terrible mistake, and con-
cluded that in the interests of the country,
and on grounds of public policy their lives
should be spared. Others also spoke, but
Carington thought himself in a crisis when,
with the daily Press with him, and, as he
foolishly fancied the great body of the public,
he was safe to exhibit a firm hand. He had
a confused Executive at his side, in a broil of
personal jealousies, and about equally divided
on the merits of the case, and as no voice for
remission came from that quarter he locked
the Prerogative up and prepared to sign
the death-warrants. But he did not sign
all the warrants. At the last minute he
exercised the Prerogative in the cases of two
and signed for the other four. Why he did
it for two and not for all is to the present
day, on serious grounds, a mystery. Sir
Henry Parkes had said that there could
not be a reprieve of one without a reprieve
of all, and that if an execution took place
the voice of the country would m the future
condemn it as a national crime. Parkes’
prophecy had not long to wait. On the very
morning of the execution, but too late of
course for Carington to see the turn of the
tide, the Sydney Morning Herald, which
had been in favour of Carington making the
law take its course, published a leading
article in which it said “ that there was no
knowing why two were reprieved,” and
further on that “ no case had been heard of in
modern times in which English judges had
compelled juries in criminal cases involving life
and death to sit all through the night in spite
of repeated protests and requests for adjourn-
ment,”
* • •
There was an opportunity which comes to
a man seldom more than once in a life and
his conduct in presence of which is the touch-
stone of his character ! Had Lord Caring-
ton, fresh from the founts of British Justice,
been an intelligent observer of legal pro-
cesses in British law-courts, he would have
recoiled, with Dalley, from the judicial
behaviour which “ had no parallel outside
the darkest period of Irish history.” But
he had not been an intelligent observer, or
his brain was unfit to weigh the com-
parison ; otherwise he would have made the
method of the trial a sufficient reason,
as it was, from the standpoint of
any man of head and heart, for the
exercise of clemency. The very confusion
and division of voices among his Executive
would, too, have been a reason. The re-
commendation to mercy unanimously made
by the jury would have been a reason. The
assurance of one of the ablest and most
responsible of the legal parliamentarians
who helped to make the law, that Parlia-
ment never intended the capital sentence to
apply to cases of the description referred to,
would have strengthened the arm of mercy.
But Carington stood stone-deaf to reason,
with a death-grip of his prerogative and
a show of firmness which was but a weak-
ling’s obstinacy. He goes now to England,
with testimonials from mere society hangers-
on, and the vapid and vain ; but with the
Royal Prerogative dyed with needlessly-shed
blood, and its prestige irrecoverably ruined
in National Australian eyes. Had ho had
the sense of a man—not to talk of the
wisdom of a statesman—he would in these
times of threatened Imperial disintegration
have, at the expense of all other popularity
if need be, held high the bright ideal of
ideal British justice, and have shielded with
the prerogative of the Queen whom he repre-
sented the victims of a prosecution s 0
obviously barbarous and so much at variance
with the sentiments of civilised humanity
as expressed in the criminal laws of Eng-
land and of every other country undominated
by the laws and prejudices of a convict-time
residuum. That—and not a record of dancing
and pleasantly dawdling with the fashion-
able, the self-serving and the bilious—
would have inspired the democracy of the
South with a thrill of admiration for the
institutions of the North. Had Governor
Carington used his prerogative, thereby
smashing a Ministry and ruining a Judge, he
might have experienced some trouble. But
law, logic, humanity, would have all been on
his side, and he would have left Australia
regarded as a strong and truly noble Eng-
lishman possessed of a stern sense of the
right of all men to a fair and impartial trial
according to both the letter and the spirit of
British law. Lord Carington is a man with
a hard heart and a soft head. That is the
sort of idol which is worshipped by society.
The Coming Confiscation.
Dear Bulletin, —One of the striking charac-
teristics of the Australian is his tendency
to form himself into an association on the
smallest pretext, and appoint a president
and a secretary, and draw up a volu-
minous report, and thereupon start out in
pursuit of some undefined object which he
neither hopes nor desires to overtake, partly
because he has no distinct impressions regard-
ing its identity, and partly because he has no
idea what to do with it. The chief business of
the association is, therefore, to hire an office
and to nominate a sub-committee, and the duty
of the sub-committee is to look into some hazy
and unspecified sub-section of the original
problem, and to announce the result of its
investigations. The Australian Single - Tax
League has already done all these things, and,
as usual, the result is practically nil. The
Land Nationalisation movement has degenerated
into a Freetrade propaganda, and as the Free-
trade party is pre-eminently the party of Land-
lordism, it follows that the great upheaval,
which was to wipe the last landlord off the face
of this continent, is now an advertising boom
for the mighty proprietor Burdekin and others
of his kind. As for the lofty cause which is
the only excuse or apology for the League’s
existence, it is only a matter of theory and
hazy speculation ; and no solitary member of
the Single-Tax Association, or of the ring of
self-seeking traders who use the Association
to rush the sale of their cheap calicoes and
their imported pants, has ever offered one prac-
tical suggestion which promises to bring the
social millennium any nearer. The lam! is one
day to belong to the people, but how the people
are to recover the land no one attempts to
decide, and a band of reformers who dare not
face the one initial difficulty of then- own
boasted reform are of no more use to the world
than the buried kings of ancient Israel. Even
the apostle of the new faith broke down
ignommiously on this cardinal point of his own
doctrine. He demonstrated eloquently enough
that the earth belonged by right to all its
inhabitants, and that its usurpation by a
handful of oligarchs was an outrage upon human
freedom, but the means by which this ancient
wrong was to be repaired he left an unknown
quantity. The choice lay between confiscation
and compensation, and as he dared not openly
commit himself to either, Ids new religion was
left unfinished. And his professed followers
have never ventured to supply this deficiency,
for which reason the Single-Tax League is now
an aimless, purposeless body, which draws up
reports about nothing, and files them away in
oblivion.
• * «
When Henry George refused either to
advocate the recovery of the land by the sum-
mary process of confiscation, or its reclamation
by purchase, he opened the way for a third
alternative: —that the whole scheme on which
the prophet’s fame and fortune were built was
merely a sham and an imposture, and an adver-
tising dodge of the meanest type. And when
the landlords and land-stealers of Sydney met
together to dine and drink and fraternise with
the iconoclast who had threatened their down-
fall, they had already recognised that he was
only an empty weapon after all. For if the land
is to be bought back by the people for the
people’s use, on the system of re-purchase
which prevails now -whenever Government
wants to re-acquire private land, then the
landlord, who runs the Government and
greases the machine of State, will him-
self conduct the sale and assess the com-
pensation, and the empty inland deserts
which were given away in the early days of
Australian settlement will suddenly rise to
fabulous prices, and dismal mountain ranges
will assume the value of suburban allotments,
and slimy swamps will undergo a boom, and the
beginning of land nationalisation will also be
the beginning of national insolvency. The
country has already had some' experience, in a
limited degree, of the landlord’s ravening hunger
after compensation. When a few inches of soil
arc wanted for railway extension then the barren
paddock which was offered the day before in the
open market for £5O, is suddenly inflated to
£2OOO, with an additional £5OO for “ deprecia-
tion” of adjoining land, and £lOO more for
damage done toa certain post-and-rail fence which
is claimed to be worth its weight in gold; and yet
the results thus arrived at by one landlord arc
only a feeble effort of arithmetic compared to
the stupendous bill which would be totted
up by all the landlords of Australia, met
together to sell out at their own estimate to a
long-suffering nation. The farewell banquet
which the monopolists of Sydney tendered to
their alleged enemy was but a feeble expression


.. titucle for the glorious vision of plunder
Viis teachings had opened out before
them- # * #

* * *
In the face of this dilemma Mr. George pro-
duced a new and emasculated edition of his
old doctrine, and tried to strike a balance
between the effusive landlord who had taken
bim in the one hand, and the people to whom
he had promised a gospel and a revelation on
the other, After denouncing for years the in-
justice of private ownership of land and calling
for the restoration of the natural rights of man-
kind, he resolved that the injustice—so far as
it had gone —must be allowed to remain, and
that the rights of mankind should slide. The
land as it stands to-day, is to be the
property of the landlord as before; there
is to be no nationalisation after all, and only
the future increment is to belong to the nation.
But unhappily, there trill be no future increment.
So long as land is a commodity for private pur-
chase and sale and barter and exchange its
price will be merely what it will fetch in the
market, and nothing more. The city allotment
which is valued at £lOOO on the day when the
new system comes into force will be sold at
£lOOO fifty years afterwards, for if any surplus
over that amount is to go to the State the land-
owner will have no desire to obtain a surplus.
The patch of ground which is now rented at
£5O will continue to be rented at £5O. The
annual value of the buildings or other improve-
ments upon it will no doubt undergo a tremen-
dous increase, for these will be strictly private
property, and the tenant will be fleeced by the
new method to exactly the same extent as he
was under the old ; but the increment which is
the property of the nation will not suffice to pay
for the working of the Act in which the great
reform is embodied. And the reason of this is
plain and easy of comprehension. An oligarchy
of landlords rules Australia, not by force of
numbers but Ivy unblushing corruption, and so
long as this oligarchy exists every law which
upholds the interests of the people at the ex-
pense of the landlord will be dexterously evaded
and set aside. To abolish monopoly Ivy statute,
and at the same time to leave the administration
of that statute in the hands of the monopolist,
is too hopeless and imbecile a scheme to deserve
serious consideration. The Foreign-trade pro-
prietors who whooped for Henry George
appreciated it at. its full value, and expressed
their gratification accordingly.
* * #
Therefore, I come back to the old alterna-
tive and advocate it boldly ; the one alternative
is Confiscation of the land—Confiscation in its
baldest, widest, most unvarnished, and most
remorseless shape. It is over this alternative
that the philanthropic landlord has waxed most
elocpvent, and, as he dwelt on its iniquity, he
has grown flabby with pathos and damp with
unshed tears. The cause which aroused his
sympathies was not his own, but that of the
struggling selector, who went forth in the early
days to do battle with Nature in her dreariest
and least inviting aspect, and his grief at the
prospective loss of his own vast estates, gained
by fraud and chicanery rather than by honest
toil, was swallowed up by his grief at the
spoliation of the industrious masses. He drew
a moving picture of this weary agriculturist
labouring for a scanty subsistence in some dusty
desert beside the Lachlan or the Darling, and
1 emanded justice on his behalf. He sketched
us forlorn surroundings—the old log residence
with a ramshackle door hanging dejectedly by
one solitary hinge, the solitary laughing-jackass
mooning on a blighted tree, the post-and-rail
ence which straddled wearily over the eternal
P am, the hashed tin and the damaged pannikin
< eposited on a stump beside the gate ; and he
pointed out to his hearers that the stay and
support of the country hung out in that unin-
uing residence, and demanded in burning
mugnation if the men who had endured hard-

items r oasis^ s °f two separate and distinct
teins~hrst, the land itself, which is compara-
tively worthless ; and second, the labour which
has created improvements upon it, and thereby
given it a new and greatly enhanced value.
The former of these will be confiscated to the
nation, and when the freehold has been ex-
changed for a leasehold, and most of the other
fiscal burdens removed, the plundered agricul-
turist will be a decided gainer by the exchange.
l>ut the destruction of the old monopoly of the
soil will fall heavily on the fatted absentee and
the millionaire proprietor—on the men who are
landlords and nothing more, who neither toil,
nor spin, nor produce anything which benefits
their fellow-men—on the class whose one busi-
ness is to gather in rents, and whose one
anxiety is to pile up the insufferable load of
their exactions. The professional landlord is
the only individual whose interests are seriously
menaced, and it is for the country to consider
whether he has ever done anything to deserve
consideration at his hands.
* # *
The Freetrade party in New South Wales
has seized upon the Land Nationalisation pro-
paganda as something exclusively its own,
under the pretext that the imposition of pro-
tective duties is essentially inconsistent with
the spirit of the movement, and having thus
borrowed the ignorant disciples of George’s
philosophy it has used them as an instrument to
carry out one half of the programme, and has
let the other half severely alone. In other
words, assisted by the votes of George’s
followers, it has successfully extinguished
local industries for the security of its
importing monopoly, and has left the masses, as
before, to bear the burden of taxation while the
landlord continues to go free. And in one
respect these softgoods apostles are correct in
their assertions : Protection certainly is incon-
sistent with the Single-Tax movement, but with
the Land Nationalisation movement it as cer-
tainly is not. The imposition of a protective
tariff combined with the abolition of land-
lordism may accidentally produce a larger
revenue than is required for the purposes of
Government, as these purposes are now under-
stood, but if so it will be only another step
upon the road of national progress. The whole
policy of modern statesmanship consists in
borrowing money and leaving the result to
futurity, and if the combination of Protection
and land-reform open a way for the extinction
of this insane method of finance there will be
abundant cause for gratitude. When the
accumulating surplus in the Treasury has made
Australia independent of the loan-mongers of
London, when it has abolished poverty by a
system of national insurance which will provide
for the old age of every citizen, and for his
children after Ids death, when it has constructed
railways and roads throughout the continent,
and reformed the foul-smelling cities where
typhoid and diphtheria accumulate, then it will
I>e time enough to raise the question of exces-
sive taxation. And then, if the Treasury is
still overflowing, the Government can be turned
into a dividend-paying concern, and distribute
a bonus of 10 per cent, among a people who
have hitherto paid much and received nothing,
and who would gladly welcome a temporary
reversal of the ancient conditions.— Titus Salt.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Kindly Offer.
The Morning Post, the favourite organ of
British Toryism and military incapacity, has
lately given birth to a new idea in which the
loyal Australian native will, no doubt, be
interested. For many years past the difficulty
of finding recruits for the British army has
been steadily on the increase. Glory, accom-
panied by beggary and a wooden leg, and the
prospect of a weary old age in the workhouse,
fails somehow to attract the incipient warrior,
and consequently the old “Shook of Gamp-
ridge ” presides over a dismal array of skeleton
battalions and paper regiments of dismounted
foot cavalry and unhorsed hussars, and every
year the forces grow smaller and beautifully
less. Under these circumstances, the Post
considers that the rampant loyalty of the
“Representative Australian” might be con-
veniently turned to account. The fatted
absentee, who lives on his rents and spends his
“ h ’’-less old age in making a painful exhibition
of himself for the benefit of “ sassiety,” is con-
spicuous for his devotion to the Crown, and as
his country has made a fearful ass of itself
before every titled individual who has visited
its shores, it is naturally assumed that this
escaped K.C.M.G. fairly represents the popular
sentiment of the nation. Hence the following
suggestion :—
Let some distinguished British regiment be quartered
in Melbourne or Sydney, and recruited from the ranks of
the stalwart Australian natives, who are far superior in
physique and soldierly qualities to the young men
enlisted at home.
This proposal, and a lot of military drivel which
accompanies it, open up a new prospect for the
“native.” Australia suffers a good deal from
attacks of martial fervour, but as yet it has
seen nothing of actual war. Its great super-
fluity of colonels and other veterans have never
experienced the joys of campaigning in some
tropical land of cholera and swamps, and
marching wearily -up interminable hills
under the weight of fifty pounds of bag-
gage in search of an invisible enemy, and
dragging over endless plains of dust and finding
nothing, and dining on bark and grass and old
tough jackal because the commissariat has
broken down, and trailing dejectedly through
eternal wildernesses in order to wipe out a
hostile Power, which consists of one dirt-caked
nigger far beyond the distant horizon. The
Australian has, unhappily, missed all these
things, and he knows nothing of the joys of
serving his beloved sovereign for one shilling a
day and a pound of bread and three-quarters of
a pound of meat, with two shirts and three
pairs of socks thrown in every seven years,
while he pays for his own underclothing, and is
charged £1 for a “sea-kit,” and is subject to
“stoppages” for tea, coffee, vegetables, salt,
pepper, milk, and sugar, and has to find his
own plate and basin, and to clean out the bar-
racks with his own soap, and to pay for library
and medical attendance, and other sundries. It
is of things like these that military glory is
compounded, but the poets who sing of war
somehow leave them out. About twice in a cen-
tury the British soldier gets a chance to have
both his legs shot off under circumstances which
live for ages in the history of his country, after
which he spends the rest of his life on crutches,
begging for coppers and being run in for
vagrancy; but in between these bursts of
glorious excitement and thrill there are long
years and years during which he scrubs floors
and brushes boots, and is an ignominious beast
of burden in a barrack on a net income of two-
pence a day and remarkably little to eat. He
has no scope for outbreaks of magnificent
heroism, and Ids patriotic fire is employed in
cleaning out the regimental dust-bin. But as
glory—especially of this order—doesn’t amount
to much unless accompanied by victuals, the
Australian has no desire to take a hand. Plain,
ordinary grub may be a subject for earth-bound
souls to contemplate, but still it is more im-
portant than fame, and it is better to have
steak and potatoes regularly than to be em-
balmed in verse and dead-broke. And, there-
fore, speaking on behalf of the “ stalwart
native,” the magnanimous offer of the Morning
Post is hereby declined with thanks.

Want of Confidence.
Last week a simultaneous vote of want of confi-
dence was moved in the New South Wales and
Victorian legislatures, and the old, weary
struggle between the men who hold office and
salary, and the men who want to hold both,
started afresh. In the Sydney Assembly, how-
ever, there is at least a definite principle
involved ; each party has a distinct fiscal policy,
and there is an important issue before the
nation ; but in the Parliament at Melbourne it
is only a question whether the old incapables
shall shuffie aimlessly along as before, or
whether the new incapables shall shuffle along
in their stead. There is no principle to be
discussed, for neither party has a principle—
there is no policy, for both parties have the same
policy, and it amounts to nothing. For fully
ten years the Victorian Legislature has drivelled
wearily over small parochial squabbles, and has
shirked every question which threatened to be
of the slightest use to humanity. Parliaments
have come and gone, and new Parliaments
have come in their stead, and crumbled
away in their turn by the incurable dry-rot of
commonplace stupidity, and still the same old
politicians have pounded out the same theories
of straw with a dismal iteration which has
neither beginning nor end. When a general
election takes place the nation merely drops its
ballot-paper in the slot and the figure moves its
jaw as before. The tremendous social struggle
which is going on throughout Australia woke
one bald-headed member from his apathy, but
when he endeavoured to arouse a passing
interest in the question the House drifted into
the refreshment-room and left him alone. The
great Federation debate which opened the
session died a natural death, because nobody
cared whether Australia was federated or not.
The national finances suddenly fell to pieces,
and the Legislature let them fall, and was too
exhausted or indifferent to pick up the fragments.
When the Assembly passed a series of resolutions
raising the pay of the railway employes the
Government drifted serenely on its way, and
took no notice ; and when it protested against
the infamous management, of the railways the
Government raised the salary of the person who
is responsible for the general muddlement, and
again nobody paid any attention. Some half-
dozen motions of censure were cast upon the
dead sea of politics, and were found again after
many days when everybody had forgotten their
existence. And now that the Gillies Ministry
are dead, the consequences remain about the
same.

Imprisonment for Debt.
Every now and then people are wakened up by
hearing of some unhappy individual who has
been arrested and confined for inability to pay
a paltry debt, and who has been left to rot and
moulder within the walls of a gaol until the
world has forgotten his existence, and he, in
his turn, has forgotten the existence of the
world, and on these occasion the Legislature
generally gives way to a little passing excite-
ment—-and does nothing. The matter, appar-
ently, lies outside the sphere of practical
politics; in other words, there is nothing in it
to steal, or blaspheme about, or to enable
one member to call another a liar, and
nobody can get a railway built on the
strength of it, or sell his ground to the
Government for eleven times its value. And as
these are the only matters which the Legisla-
tures really care about it naturally follows that
Mr. Bibbs’ motion in the New South Wales
Parliament for the abolition of this
ancient infamy was ignominiously shelved, and
the Bill which the Pahkes Government under-
took to introduce in its stead is postponed,
apparently, till the Greek Kalends. The law
of imprisonment for debt is supported by the
united force of Capital, for it enables the
capitalist to wreak a mean revenge on his
enemy as no other statute could do. It is a
scheme under which a citizen can be confined
indefinitely without a trial or a hearing of any
kind, and under its influence poverty is a more
fatal crime than forgery or assault with violence.
No one, of course, is shut up because he
is unable to pay a debt, for the law is
based on Christian principles, and to sentence
a man to imprisonment—perhaps for life—
because he is without money would be as
impious as to hang him for the same cause ; he
is merely sliut up for “ contempt ” till he pays
the debt, but despite t he glamour of hypocrisy
thus thrown over the proceeding, the result,
somehow, is exactly the same. No one is ever
enabled, through being shut up, to settle a
liability which he was unable to liquidate while
he was at large ; it would be as rational to hope
that flogging would serve as a cure for baldness,
or to chain up a black man with a view to mak-
ing him white. The measure is not in-
tended as a remedy for indebtedness, for
even the Law recognises the fact that
a man in gaol is much less able to pay his
liabilities than a man at liberty ; neither is it
meant as a punishment, for the code does not
acknowledge financial embarrassment as a
felony ; it is merely an unconstitutional act of
personal revenge which the usurer is allowed to
exercise upon his victim, and a partial survival
of the old system of slavery, under which the
debtor became the personal property of tile
creditor, to lie sold or otherwise dealt with at
his discretion, ft is one of those legal pitfalls
which serve to entrap the poor ignor-
ant ; the unscrupulous speculator whose debts
amount to tens of thousands lias never been im-
prisoned for debt within the memory of man, and
the wealthy swindler who will come under the
operation of this law is still unborn. The man
who drags out years in a debtors’ gaol Is
generally some foolish individual who failed
to realise in time that it was much cheaper to
steal £IO,OOO and be esteemed for ever after-
wards than to make an honest endeavour to pay
£2O and fail in the attempt. His liabilities, as
a rule, are a mere trifle, for a man who under-
stands the art of owing large sums of money
invariably hires a brainy lawyer to see him
through Ins difficulties, and gets his discharge
without paying anybody. He is also, in most
cases, a well-meaning and idiotically honour-
able individual, whose Christian stupidity pre-
vented him departing with all his available
assets for regions unknown. And his fate serves
as a solemn warning to men who have hitherto
attempted to be honest on a small, unobtrusive
scale, and have suffered from a morbid craving
to keep square with the world, whereas they
ought to have borrowed in an oily way from
everybody under the sun and floated bills all
over the habitable globe, and then cursed their
creditors from bed-time to breakfast.

“The proposition of the N.S.W. Attorney-
General,” says the S.M. Herald, “ to provide
legal assistance for pauper prisoners is so indis-
putably right in principle that the only wonder
is that it was not made long ago.” The reform
is, apart from the Attorney-General and his
colleagues, solely and wholly due to The Bul-
letin, which has for years past used the case of
every undefended but rigorously-prosecuted
citizen to advocate the necessity of this socialistic
measure. The Sydney Morning Herald
applauds the innovation— hut, it has never
advocated it ! Whatever is, is right according
to the S.M. Herald. It was right all through
the 50 years during which the S.M. Herald has
been in existence as a daily paper, to allow the
penniless pauper accuse to face his doom, some-
times with absolutely no one to say a word for
him or cross-examine the witnesses against him,
while under the most favourable circumstances
he had to depend on the gratuitous advo-
cacy, undertaken perhaps at half-an-hour’s
notice, of some stammering, well-intentioned
boy, or hopeless legal dead-beat. Now it
is right that the pauper should be pro-
perly defended at State expense ! We do
not often care to publicly claim credit for big
reforms of which we have sown the seeds, but
cannot help pointing out that we lately drew
attention to the disgracefully tardy provision
made for the defence of the murderer, Smidt,
and that the week immediately prior to the
announcement that State funds would, when-
ever necessary, in future be available for the
defence of capitally-arraigned prisoners, we
remarked upon the fact that an undefended
man had that week been tried for his life at
Darlinghurst, and notified on i intention of
thereafter invariably taking steps to collect
funds for all capitally-arraigned undefended
prisoners unless the State should come to the
rescue. The State has come to the rescue, and
the act is one for which every lover of humanity
and equal justice to all men will from the
bottom of his heart thank the Parkes Ministry.
Henry Varley, the alleged evangelist, take*
up his cross and testifies against the sinfulness
of Democracy and Trades Unionism ;
I observe that the lowering of entrance fees to the re
spective unions is taking place, and forbearance with
non-unionists advocated in order to the rapid absorption
of all workers into the ranks of trades unions. I trust
that these efforts will not succeed. The result could only
be a colossal monopoly, at once exacting, tyrannous, anil
oppressive.
Just so. When Unionism has combined all the
workers in one compact body then a gigantic
monopoly will be formed consisting of the entire
nation, and as the evangelist only appreciates a
monopoly when it is made up of a few capita-
lists he feels mad accordingly. A monopoly,
as he justly observes, is always tyrannous,
when it can find anybody to tyrannise over,
therefore the existing monopoly of land-
sharks, importers, landlords, and syndicates
tyrannises over the rest of the popula-
tion. Should the balance of power change
and all the workers combine to keep the
authority in their own hands, then it is pro-
bable that they will oppress the gilded loiter in
their turn ; in fact, there will be no one else to
oppress, for the worker will certainly not
oppress himself. Mankind is always on the
lookout for a chance to oppress somebody, and'
so long as there is any human nature left in
humanity this aggressive habit will not* die out.
Therefore, man has to decide between two
alternatives—either the worker on whom the
world depends for its prosperity, is to be at
liberty to oppress the idler, who is useless as
well as offensive, or else the idler must con-
tinue to oppress the worker as heretofore. In
other words, it is a question whether, out of
every thousand human beings, there is to be one
monopolist and 90!) slaves, or whether 990 men
are to share the monopoly and leave to the odd
survivor the alternative of either becoming a
worker like the rest, or an outcast. It has to
be decided whether man was made for the
landlord and the capitalist, or not, and if he
was, then it seems almost a pity that anvone
ever took the trouble to make him at all.

Visiting a London workhouse a pressman
asked an old man who was employed among a
long row of black coffins : “ How long he had
been there?” “Fifteen years,” he answered.
“ What have you been doing all that time?”
“Making coffins for paupers,” he said, “and,” lie
added, “ I was just a-wishing I could make one for
myself.”

Ask for Wolfe’s Schnapps and take no other.
oilier half-starved tribe which could produce
low-priced shoddy by slave labour, and thereby
deprive the American workman of His employ-
ment. The Freetrade lecturer and the Free-
trade press have both whooped incessantly
about the 50,000,000 enlightened citizens who
were about to join the great cause; and when
the corpulent Cleveland attained the Prosi-,
dential chair and issued his famous manifesto it
was resolved that the end of Protection was at
hand. Even when Cleveland was indignantly
sacked front his high office, and Ids triumphant
party was reduced to hopeless ruin and smash
by their leader's attempt to destroy American,
industry for the .benefit of a ring of importers
the hymn of rejoicing went on as before,
and the advent of Harrison with an
almost unanimous Protection isl legislature
at bis back was described as being
merely the hour of darkness which goes
before the dawn. lint the passing of (be
new Tariff Act reveals the fact that there, is
actually no Freetrade party in the United
States at all. Even Cleveland has long since
apostatised and explained himself away, and -
barring Henry Ceorue -there is nothing left.
Tin; great reaction declines to start. Every
nation which has tried the grand old nostrum
positively declines to try it again. America,
France, Germany, and Spain have all experi-
mented with it in turn and have abandoned it
unanimously, and even England, where, a few
years ago, Protection was as extinct as the
mammoth or the moa, is growing weary of
poverty and hunger and low wages, and wants
to know why all her goods seem to hear the
trade-mark, “made in Germany.” The Free-
trade politician rests his case entirely on the
assumption that one-half the population of New
South Wales and about three-fourths that of
England are in exclusive possession of the light
of truth, while all the rest of the world is in
darkness, and he kindly volunteers to strike a
match and illuminate the earth. But somehow
even when he strikes his match the earth fails
to take any notice of the circumstance.
The standard height of the British soldier is
now sft. 41n., his chest measurement 38 inches,
and weight 115 lbs., and even on this scale 47
per cent, of recruits ape rejected on medical
examination, while a large number never even
reach This test, but are summarily disposed of
by the recruiting officer as hopelessly unfit for
duty. The cheap freetrade loaf, made of im-
ported flour, which has all hut extinguished
the stalwart peasantry of England, is fast
reducing the nation to a mere gathering of
hollow-chested weavers and cotton-spinners,
and already the utter impossibility of finding
fighting material for the defence of the country
has led one Tory journal to suggest that London
should be garrisoned by Sepoys from Bengal
and by Sikh regiments from the land of the
Five River’s, while another wants to know if
Australia cannot be turned into a recruiting
ground for the benefit of a decayed race which
has sold its sons and daughters for the sake
of ready cash. Tn the old days England grew
tired of Protection, because under its auspices
the Tory landlord raised the price of bread and
exploited the nation for his own benefit. Then,
instead of abolishing the landholder—the
principal cause of the prevailing hunger and
scarcity—she invented the bastard system of
Freetrade, and thereby abolished the agricul-
tural population ; in other words, she attempted
to extinguish oppression by letting the oppressor
go and hanging his victim so that he might
escape further injury. It was a strange, in-
verted, upside-down policy, and the wildly-
gifted genius who invented it would probably—
working on the same basis-—have stunned a
rheumatic patient with a club to prevent him
feeling pain, or recommend flogging as an
appropriate cure for hidden grief, inasmuch as
the flogger’s mind would thereby be diverted to
other considerations; but Britain has never
altogether realised the stupendous dimensions
of her own fatuity, and when she fairly com-
prehends the depth and breadth and thickness
of that unexampled blunder which she made
some fifty years ago, it will be too late to
remedy the evil.

81C Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Novembers, 1890,
My Dear Moorahixda,—
The Citizens’ valedictory dinner to GovernorCarington,
at Sydney Centennial Hal), on 28th ult., was a thrilling,
spectacle in a halo of pot plants, and if, perhaps, the
balloon-view from the balconies did occasionally include
tilted soup-plates, dinner-napkins tucked under double
chins, and little green peas ranged along divers glittering
blades—still it was a thrilling spectacle. The board of
honour was set across the top of the hall (with the band
immediately behind, by the way, strumming “ Paul
Jones,” two feet off the tired gubernatorial tympana), and
the Guest, in evening dress with a ribbon order, satat the
right of the Mayor (in official robes) who had Gov. Hope-
toun on his left. Sir Henry Norman at Lord Carington’s
right, the sonsie Kintore, who was as pink as a shrimp, to
the left of the Governor of Victoria, with Cardinal Moran
in his picturesque robes on his left, while the Bishop of
Sydney, who was attired very rustily, was placed to
the right of Chief Justice Parley. The decorations on
the chief table were like an explosion in a dye-factory, and
the stiff fern-leaves tacked about on the hanging cloth
had the effect of cold tea-leaves from above. There
didn’t seem to be enough waiters to go round, and the
frantically floral menus were too sober in colouring. It
was a sort of stork’s feast for the Guest, too, who was
wisely temperate and nibbled rolls principally, and I
distinctly saw the distinguished victim make a
small grimace over his champagne. The orators
were very audible with the exception of Sir
Henry Parkes, whose neatly - proposed toast of
the “ Governor ” sounded like George Leitch heard
per phonograph, and everyone was glad, so far as mere
sound went, when Dibbs .took up the parable in his most
degage manner. Lord Hopetoun made a “ daisy ”
oration, as such orations go, though his calisthenics
suggested that somebody pulled a string to work the
figure, ar.d Lord Oarington’s statistical, sentimental and
non-committal adieu was received with much after-
dinner cheering and a snowstorm of waving serviettes.
Indeed, the enthusiasm all through suggested to your
Sappho that there is nothing like champagne in large
quantities for assuring three sustained howls for anything
under the sun.
If the scene on the floor was on the whole sombre and
suggested a inass-meeting of magpies, the balconies came
nobly to the scenic rescue, for though there were hundreds
of women present, all—save half-a-dozen—were in full war.
paint. Lady Carington, who was welcomed by a perfect
uproar and much waving of handkerchiefs, was accom-
panied by Lady Kintore, who sat at her left, Lady
William Robinson, and young Lord Inverurie, who
anticipated his fate by appearing in an Eton suit. The
vice-regal dress was distinctly special. Lady Carington
wore a trained dress in ivory-white satin brocade with
skirt panel of pearled net; elbow and sleeves of white net
and crystal beads, the turned-down collar lined with
llsse ; diamond ornaments, white gloves, and nosegay
(tied with blue ribbons) of cream roses, forget-me-not
lilies of the valley and fern. Lady Kintore sported a gown
in buttercup brocade draped over cream-silk, the
train having a kind of Centre panel of cream-silk
edged with two rows of gold galon; brocade bodice
with Maude Millet fichu in white chiffon, nosegay of
cream and crimson carnations, diamond pin in the hair;
Lady Robinson appeared in black brocade, the jupe of
jetted net, sleeves of striped jetted net, cluster of white
roses on the corsage, black gloves, row of pearls and a
hand of black velvet round the throat, pearl-and-diamond
crescent in the hair, diamond pendant, Japanese fan.
Then Mrs. Burdekin (the hostess of the galleries) wore
heliotrope armure with the jupe draped in fine lace,
jouane bodiee over a full front of lace, lace sleeves,
diamond necklet, cluster of white flowers on the bodice,
and nosegay in pale tints. Lady Innes wore chocolate-
brown and marigold, the sleeves of yellow silk; Miss
Saumaurez Smith, striped black silk with full sleeves of
black gauze, mantle in w’hite satin brocade with Medici
collar; Miss Broomfield, smoke-grey tulle and brocade,
with trails of scarlet poppies and scarlet fan ; Miss Nellie
Hill, pale-pink and apple-green silk. Most of the on-
lookers wore cream, pink or blue frocks, and so helped
the aesthetic aspect immensely. Coffee, cake and ices
circulated around the galleries, and a buffet was laid in
one of the chambers, where people refreshed themselves
on the way out. The gubernatorial ladies, the Mayoress,
and a great many others, left when the cigars began to
circulate below.
The bouquets carried by the vice-regal trinity were of
the smallest description—at most a dozen flowers bunched
anyhow, so, of course, tiny posies are now all the thing.
Men and women are always florally antagonistic. The
fashionable masculine button-hole is the size of a soup-
plate.
The valedictory public schools concert to Lord and Lady
Carington, held at Centennial Hall on 20th ult., was a
successful, crowded-out affair, where mothers waved long
limp handkerchiefs at the infants on the platform, and
proud fathers encouraged their darlings on the wild high.
C with ear-splitting handclaps. The platform, peopled
mostly with girl choristers all in white dresses with either
crimson or blue scarves, seated in rows one above the
other, the first row holding floral baskets, was most effec-
tive from the front. The s'nging (1500 voices) was pleas-
ing, but the cadets’ vocal march would have looked better
if boys of one height had been chosen instead of being so
selected as to run down in a scoop in the middle (however,,
they were most likely chosen for vocal qualities); and
the “ Federated Australia ” tableau, where young girls
representing the various provinces laid grain, fruit,
flowers, &c., at the feet of a typical Australia (Miss Alpon)i
formed a very effective picture.
The vice-regal party arrived punctually at eight Lady
Carington wore'a dinner dress of pale-blue, with folded
bodice and cluster of plush roses and leaves held by a
diamond star, diamonds in the hair, and came in with
Minister Carruthers, who carried the presentation bouquet
in cream roses tied with blue streamers. Lord Carington,
who seemed to be expanding visibly under the influence
of his near reprieve, brought Mrs. Carruthers in white
silk with black velvet braces and shoulder-knots and
cluster of pink roses. The vice-regal party included Mrs.
Windeycr and Miss Windeyer, who were in blue silk and
white lace fichu ; Mrs. Burdekin, in sunflower silk and net
spangled with gold sequins, corsage bouquet of crimson
roses; Sir Frederick Barley, and the Misses Barley in
black gowns; Mrs. Alfred Allen (hon. secretary to the
concert committee), in black velvet gown with cluster of
saffron roses ; and one or two others.
Last of all the farewell festivities was the complimen-
tary, ball to Lady Carington on 30th tilt, in the Centennial
Hall when the strange, weird element was largely elimin-
ated (as I predicted) though your Sappho did see a lady
sharing her chair with a gentlemen at the supper-tabic
and, one unconventional couple taking a short cut across
the dais while their “Excellencies” were in possession.
Nearly a thousand, attended, and there was a soothing
surplus of men that was very pleasant for everybody' but
the bifurcated wall-flowers themselves. The decorations
wore arranged on the same principle as the club balls,
immense palms everywhere, festoons of greenery and
wreaths of Lady Oarington’s favourite octiuotus*
and two little singing canaries were suspended on either
side of the dais. An excellent supper was served
in the lower chamber, and the table-decorations—large
hearts in cream and crimson roses were gasp-
ingly nice. And so were some of the dresses. Lady
Carington (who left rather early considering the ball was
held in her honour) wore heliotrope silk, chiffon, and
wisteria, diamond ornaments, heliotrope shoes, and im-
mense committee bouquet; Mrs. Burdekin, pcach-pink,
surah, and chiffon, with deep ruche of frayed silk round
the feet, caught with pale-pink flowers, diamond brooches
and necklace. Lady Innes wore a charming turn-out —
the train of striped buttercup silk, the front trimmed and
draped with fine tinted lace, sleeves and bodice trimming
of chiffon caught with yellow bows, magnificent diamonds.
Miss Burdett Smith appeared in white brocade, pearl
trimming, diamond necklace; Mrs. Carruthers, in ivory
silk en traine, black plume fan ; the Misses Holliday,
in yellow and white floral gowns respectively; Miss Bowker,
sap-green moire, shoulder-puffs of green moire with
pink chiffon butterflies, pink plume fan ; Lady Martin’s
diamonds lit up her gown of black-corded silk and jetted
lace. Miss Martin wore pale-blue tulle over satin and
clusters of saffron roses, bouquet of pink roses ; Miss
Frances Saville, jupe of cream brocade, train of pom-
padour brocade in pink and blue edged with pink and
blue ribbon tabs; Miss Barley, an effective frock of
black and white tulle ; Mrs. Yanderveer Greene sported
a Mephistophiles gown that carried all before it. And, let
me add, while among the “un-nervers,” that a scarlet
bag frock trimmed with villainously green fringe ; a pink
satin greenaway gown on a lady considerably over seven,
and the large, imposing creature in slick white satin that
increased her width to amazement-point, most harrowed
the instincts of your slim Sappho.
Mr. Percy Spence has commenced his contribution to
the Australian artists’collection to be sent “Home.” It
is a large canvas, to be called “To Fresh Fields and
Pastures new,” and will represent a weary shepherd lead-
ing bis horse and following a flock of prime merinoes
along-a very special thing in country roads.

Majesty's, needs all the light and colour the management
can conveniently' throw at it, hence it is pleasing to find
that even madness doesn’t prevent a woman from ordering
the latest thing in hats from the milliner, and insisting
on having her shoes en suite. Kate Bishop—who, by the

and shoes, and a picture hat in grey gauze and feathers
with a knot of pink in the crown ; Watts-Phillips is
in cream delaine figured Indian red, and Indian red rib.
bons tied in front. In the first act, Watts-Phillips
wears leaf-brown polonaise with sleeves, collar, and under-
skirt of brown silk striped with black velvet; rough straw
hat smothered in roses. In act two, Kate Bishop appears

dress edged with chiffon frills over a brocade jupe, a trail
of hedge-roses and leaves falling from the right shoulder
on to the skirt, elbow sleeves of chiffon open in front I

cups and leaves and trimmed with a cluster of hedge
roses—Watts-Phillips contrasting pale-blue silk chiffon
and passementerie with this handsome gown. In act
three Pauline returns to reason in a white mull pleated

tuce fringe and tied at the waist with a scarf edged with
blue fringe; trained over-dress of pale-blue silk with a

blue shoes. Watts-Phillips here wears a charming dress,
the jupe of pink chiffon edged with gold, the train of
pink armure, high shoulder puffs and scarf-tying in front
and hanging at the edge of the skirt of pink chiffon
finished with gold tassels, the front of the bodice embroi-
dered in gold.
I read that “the very latest fashion is to have the
visiting-card with a raised border,” and possibly some
foolish novelty-hunter may be induced to take it up
under a mistaken idea that the plain, square, paste-card
as thin as a wafer no longer remains the correct label lo
state your identity on. By the bye, I was cpnfronted the
other day with “ mourning ” paper with a sort of rakish
black cap across the right-hand upper corner of paper
and envelope. I was told that it was the “latest fashion ’’
and I tried to look as if I believed it, but it was a great
deal too dashing for the circumstances under which one
uses mourning stationery, and will only be utilised by the
hopelessly vulgar. Yet there are some unthinking people
who take a staring eccentricity on its own value just
because it is retailed by a “good ” shop.
One change “Wales” will notice about “Charlie” is
that he has now to hoist pince-nez when confronted with
print. But it might have been worse, for the gig-lamps
quite suit the Baron. Considering Lord Carington’s
juxtaposition to music at every social outburst for the
last five years, it’s a Wonder he isn’t forging homewards
with an ear-trumpet in his strong right hand.

The heart of M. Gambetta is missing. Its
history is an interesting one. When the post-
mortem examination of the remains of the great
Frenchman was made, under the direction of M.
Paul Bert, the eminent scientist and intimate
friend of Gambetta’s, when that superficial wound
in the arm which we all heard so much about, and
the fatal wound in the abdomen about which noth-
ing was said, had been probed, the heart of the
murdered statesman was taken from the body and
carried away by M. Bert for examination. In his
possession it remained for some years, and was
shown frequently to curious visitors at the labora-
tory. Four or five years ago M. Bert sent the
precious curiosity to Cahors, the birthplace ot
Gambetta, on the occasion of the unveiling ot a
statue of the statesman. Since that date it has
never been seen. M. Paul Bert, it will be reniem
bered, died suddenly in the East. He had always
said that he would have the heart of his fnenc
placed beneaththemagnificent Gambetta inetnona
in the Palace du Carrousel, but it never was P ut
there, because it never could be found.

“A Night Off,” at Sydney Criterion, went
with a loud bang on opening night. The curtain
had to be drawn up three times at the close of the
third act on George Anson’s toga scene, and then,
the enthusiasm continuing, that kilted person was
compelled to take a call before the curtain. Anson
{his humour is not so broad as of yore) and Annie
Taylor are as good as ever in their original parts,
but the play all round is not so strongly cast as
when first produced at the Opera House. Made
up to resemble a washed-out and pared-down
Abraham Lincoln, the Professor Babbit of Robert
Brough is an undignified, idiotic, and weak-kneed
person for whom Rabbit would have been a more
truly descriptive cognomen. Mrs. Robert Brough,
like her predecessor, is scarcely girly-girly enough
as Nisbe; Emma Bronton is not sufficiently
imperious as the termagent Xantippa; Nellie
Mortyne does not suffer by comparison with the
original Angelica; Cecil Ward is scarcely light
enough as the Mercutio-Hke Mulberry; and Owen
Harris, as Damask (made up to resemble Tither-
adge) does not seem at home as a goody-goody
young man. Smaller parts—mere excrescences
that might with advantage be lopped off—are
creditably filled by E. C. Corlesse and W. F.
Olitherow. This play is in for a good run.
# * *
It is likely that the feeling of disappoint-
ment produced by Bellew’s Romeo is chiefly due
to the fact that he looks the character so
thoroughly that a high standard of anticipation is
set up only to be demolished when that stagey
person begins attitudinising and mouthing. There-
fore it is that his performance is less satisfactory
than the weaker impersonations of predecessors less
gifted physically and sartorially; therefore it is
that no eye is moist when a draught of “Rough
on Romeo ” takes him off quickly. The quarrel
scene with the nurse is Mrs. Potter’s best effort as
Juliet, and recognising that her lady of one love
has failed to hit Sydney Royal audiences hard,
that astute and business-like charmer will, on
Saturday, begin to impersonate “Camille,” the
lady of many loves, for three nights. “ She Stoops
To Conquer” will be put up next Wednesday and
on next Saturday “ Hero and Leander ” will take
the boards for a fortnight, and the season will
probably conclude with the devotion of two nights
to “La Tosca,” “Frou-Frou,” and “David
Garrick. ”
** * *
“Called Back,” at Sydney Her Majesty’s*
has so tickled the public palate, that “The
Merry Wives of Windsor” may not be required to
fill the gap between now and pantomime time.
The charm of the Macari of George Rignold—
an oleaginous, wheedling, sensual, perfidious
animal (reminiscent of an Italian tenor we once
met), is greatly due to the great gap that exists
between it and his usual direct, almost brusque,
manner. Anyhow, the impersonation has pro-
cured for him a renewed lease of popularity.
Manager Macdonald is gradually gaining strength
and though still a little shaky, has now assumed
full command in front.
** * *
At Sydney School of Arts, Harry Rickards
faces the music nightly with several new songs,
including “Nevermore,” a ditty likely to be
popular. The company has been strengthened,
numerically at least, by the engagement of Alma
Westleigh, a singer just arrived from London,
whose voice, small but sweet, has been carefully
trained, but whose method is too quiet for music-
hall business ; Emmie Morrison, a dainty little
Tyrolean warbler; E. B. Hogg, an invertebrate
contortionist ; and Frank Hawthorne, the Aus-
tralian mimic, whose imitations of Maccabe,
Greville and Bill Holloway, in particular, are
very good.

ninth afternoon orchestral concert will take place
in the Sydney Town Hall, when Lilian Tree will
expand her lungs and fill that reverberating
edifice with sweet sounds.

Ibbotfc, the only “man of mark” who did
not attend any of the Caringtonian banquets,
continues “to eat into himself for lack of some-
thing else to hew and hack,” at the rate of one
pound-and-a-half daily, and has now lost nearly
two stones in weight inside 14 days. It is a cer-
tainty that more sickness and probably misery is
caused by intemperate eating than intemperate
drinking, and we notice with pain that most of the
uever-touch-its never attempt to curb their
gluttony, and if not martyrs to dyspepsia are
eventually the victims of gout and apoplexy.
Jawson, of Birmingham, who died not long ago,
once published a treatise showing how life
might be sustained on sixpence a day, and
Colonel Fitzgibbon, a seventy-year-old London
a gent of the Canadian Government, whose remit-
tances were delayed, with a sovereign in hand
ived for a month on four pennyworth of bread,
one pennyworth of milk, and one pennyworth of
sugar daily, and had five shillings in hand when
tue money came along. The old party throve so
tl® i° n this diet, the pap of his second childhood,
nat he persevered in it for two years longer, and
evoted the cash thus saved to the establishment
ana support of infant-schools in the poorer quar-
.-°f London. Now, we want to know what
patriot is going to do with all the tucker he hasn’t
eaten at the expiration of the forty days ?

** * *
“ The English Rose ” started its blooming
career to big business at Melbourne Royal—a
house where a drama by any other name would
smell as sweet these holiday times. Sims and
Buchanan wrote “The English Rose” between
them, and the result is not particularly happy.
The poet seems to have aimed at producing a
serious Irish play, which the rhymester was to
lighten, but neither of them did his portion of the
work so well as he might have accomplished the
whole lot single-handed. Moreover, the central
idea of a priest finding himself the human
envelope of a ghastly secret under the confes-
sional seal has been already presented on the
Australian stage and savagely worried by the
press until there is no life left in it. Likewise the
circumstance of a supposed murderer being
rescued from the law is painfully reminiscent of
aii alleged Irish drama recently produced by
O’Grady. Also the finest pisantry in the world
shoot around exactly as they did a few weeks ago,
which is decidedly monotonous of them, and
finally the hero appears to pop up on the very
same horse that used to carry George Darrell’s
“ double ” over a five-barred gate. In London,
doubtless, the horse has been an interesting fea-
ture, but in Melbourne he comes as a drug on the
market just now. With Carbine fresh in their
minds’ eyes audiences have little or no respect for
the stage quadruped. By the same token, they
cannot pump up excitement over an invisible
steeplechase which seems to be contested in a
shrubbery at the wings. This sporting event may
stir the characters in the drama to their very
souls, but it falls flat on the characters who have
just returned from races they saw and had their
money on. Points in favour of “ The English
Rose ” are, firstly, its pretty and solid scenery, by
Brunton, Goatcher, and Hennings; secondly, its
freedom from brogue. The authors have avoided
comparisons with Boucicault, and nobody says
“ begorra ” to any serious extent, or inquires after
his mavourneen acushla and so forth. Quid
Oireland is, in fact, a good deal like London as
regards its inhabitants.
# • #
The now arrivals from “ home ” don’t add
materially to the acting strength of the company.
Laurence Cautley is a small cut of hero with no
apparent fund of dramatic force, and Harold
Russell is a stage villain to match. Miss Mary
Kingsley, however, seems to bring every qualifi-
cation except experience and confidence —a tall,
pretty young creature, strictly fresh and uncon-
ventional in style and manner of speaking. The
honours of the performance fall to Clyndes, who
comes out in the strongest part, while
Arthur Lawrence is sufficiently good as the priest,
and Christine Darrell plays a love-lorn melo-
dramatic female in a strange spirit. J. R.
Greville’s humour has not been affected, one way
or the other, by foreign travel, and a lot of other
people are generally speaking up to the mark, or
somewhere near it.
* * *
“Paul Jones” was received with open
arms at the Opera House, and when the mariners
bold chirped a shaky verse to the effect that they
had come back to stay the gallery betrayed strong
symptoms of joy. Nevertheless the mariners
didn’t seem such salt sea dogs as they were afore-
time, and if they bought a few fresh wheezes with
those bags of bullion, instead of throwing them at
Hudson, nobody would regret the investment.
Otherwise the show has lost very little of its old
attraction, although three changes from the
original cast bring nothing more than novelty to
the scene. Fanny Liddiard’s voice sounds thin
after the Burtonic organ tones, still she scores a
point by looking uncommonly like Kyrlo Bellew
at the end of the second act, Lena Salinger fits
herself fairly well with the sabots of Aggie Kelton,
and the Spanish lady with a red face can easily
divest herself of her superfluous colour.
** * *
“TheGondoliers,”at Melbourne Princess’,
is already the talk of the city, and will presumably
turn out to be the richest reef of Gilbert and
Sullivan that the management have struck.
Plenty of opportunities for hurling marmalade at
this gorgeous entertainment will offer themselves
as it rolls along its course, with more than one
member of the number hanging on behind. In
the meantime, a number of first-night patrons
will be relieved at hearing that Flora Graupner
has discarded the scarlet stockings which brought
her limbs too immediately under critical notice,
and has put them into deep mourning instead.
This is a change in the right direction.
** * *
“ Harvest,” at the Bijou, makes a deep
impression on that section of the community
which carries a heart into the theatre and yearns
for something more than a comic song, or a gun-
powder explosion. The story is apt, however, to
set some people writhing, and every now and then
a bulbous-nosed savage, who came in expecting to
see something funny, will glance uneasily at the
door as though fearful of a policeman swooping
down upon him with a warrant issued by one of
the sixteen wives cast off by him (the bulbous-
bokoed one) at various periods of his history.
“Harvest” is not the right play for individuals
of this stamp, who invariably slink away after the
prologue and sodden their consciences in rum. On
Saturday the B. and B, company appear in “ Im-
pulse,” with Maud Williamson and Fanny Enson
playing their original London characters, and
Titheradge back in the old place.
* & %
Down at the Alexandra things looks cheer-
ful for “The Land of the living,” where th»
“ visional effects’’ are understood to be run on
Pepper’s Ghost principles. There are two notable
touches of nature in this drama. One is a wash-
tub in which Ada Lee purifies the villain’s alter-
native shirt whilst moralising over the troubles of
wedded life. The other touch of nature is pro-
vided by a garden spade, wherewith the aforesaid
villain administers what appears to be a ghastly
crack across somebody’s skull. The spade and the
blow both appear genuine enough. Perhaps the
skull, which belongs to Walter Howe, has been
specially prepared for the occasion.
** * *
The Exhibition Hippodrome has rapidly
degenerated in public esteem, but at latest dates a
parachutist was ascending from the Wild West in
a balloon and descending upon chimney stacks and
church-steeples. As there seems a reasonable
chance of this intrepid individual meeting his
death business ought to improve. The worst of it
is that people can stand outside the entertainment
and see the gentleman die gratis. Other shows
are thick in Melbourne this week. At St. George’s
Hall they have the Phonograph, a Circus is some-
where about, the Waxworks and Cyclorama do
well, as a matter of course, and the two variety
shows at Gaiety and Victoria Halls get their shares
of the wandering multitude.
* * -* -*
A little slab of Yankee criticism regarding
the well-known Fay Templeton: “ The coming
Fay was ethereal, pensive, sprite-like. The
arrived Fay was adipose, somewhat brazen. The
promised notoriety basked in the freshness and
swelled with the exuberance of youth. Her very
calves were vernal. The furnished notoriety
capered heavily in the light of other days, and her
protuberant physique was autumnal. One may
acknowledge on behalf of the theatre-going public
that the half-disclosed perfections of a sylph are
not without a fervour-stirring charm. But one
does not pare to see a dowager in tights. And
dowagerhood betrays itself not only in black silk
and side curls, but in fleshly lines. A sylph with
a stomach may be possible, but it is tedious. A
sylph may pose, but Heaven spare us when she
adiposes !,’ It is the pathetic old story over again.
There is no place in all this world for the fat,
elderly heroine, and earth has no fury like a cor-
pulent female scorned.

Manager Williamson has embellished the
dress-circle entrance to Melbourne Princess’ with
choice frames of autographed photos of Irving,
the Bancrofts, ’Gus Harris, &o. Augustus
Druryolanus has thickened almost out of know-
ledge and with a beard of formal cut he looks the
Oriental alderman all over. Only a few years
ago he was playing dashing young heroes in
dramas, and now he wears a beard ! Well, they
certainly were hair-raising productions.

Some clever artistes are very unfortunate in
the time they strike a place. Janauschek, a really
talented actress, was here at the same time as
Ristori, and Kowalski followed close on the heels
of Ketten. Both Janauschek and Kowalski
suffered.

A crowd of amateurs attracted large and
fashionable audiences at the Adelaide Royal for
two nights, the performances being for a deserving
charitable object. Eille Norwood—actor, author
and whistler —was at the head of affairs, and
the programme consisted of his comedietta,
“ Hook and Eye,” and Byron’s “Weak Woman.”
Harry St. Maur is now doing fair business with
“Jim the Penman,” supported by Florence
Seymour, David Christie Murray, Fred. Neebe
and others. “ The Candidate” follows. “ Dick”
Whittington, the theatre treasurer, is promised a
benefit shortly.

The Leicester Magistrates recently refused a license for
the appearance of little May Hannan in “ Hans the Boat-
man,” on the ground that her education was being
neglected. An idiotic decision, which reminds us that
Arnold was lucky to make his £5OOO in Australia—mostly
in Melbourne—before the Victorian law against infant
prodigies was enforced. A policeman would have to walk
on the stage and arrest May Hannan if she were appear-
ing at the Bijou nowadays.
Playing “ Chawles” at Dunedin Princess’—with Jimmy
Moore in management for W. and G. —Toole successfully
worked off his old clothes-gag, wherein, while wearing
the livery of a very much bigger man, a leg of the knee-
breeches drops to his ankle, and, he pretending it is an
accident, the people roar at his apparent misfortune and
simulated embarrassment. It is the veriest horse-play
and would deceive only a novice, nevertheless the Otago
Daily Times critic—not knowing what buffoonery this
high-class comedian ( vide S.M. Herald) is capable of—
devotes a-flfth of a column to praising “the singular
adroitness of the apposite remarks with which Mr. Toole
passed the mishap off after ineffectually endeavouring by
closing Ms legs to check the downward movement.” They
are awfully green at Dunedin, and that’s just how Toole
likes ’em. Worse than all, some of the stupid people on this
side, who should know better, clip the item.

An Australian medico having surreptitiously won the
heart of a “ private ” barmaid visiting his city in the care
of a theatrical manager, was surprised to receive a visit
from the showman, who thanked him for taking a some-
what passtie article off his hands, and placed a season
ticket for two at his disposal. The'lady, with her sex’s
hankering after the theatrical, not to be got rid of so
easily, threatens the showman with a breach of promise
action, and he, fearing exposure, knows not what to do.

benefit ?
Vesta Tilley, the music-hall artiste who lately became
Mrs. de Frece and has been engaged as principal boy for

ence of some weeks declares that “marriage is a
tremendous hit and likely to have a long run.”
It is said that Cardinal Newman once sent a note to
Braham—the great Jewish tenor, advertised to take part
in the annual "musical services at St. Mary’s for the benefit
of the Radcliffe Infirmary—interdicting his singing.
Braham took no notice ; at the appointed time in the pro-
gramme he stood up in the organ gallery, and filled the
church with his magnificent voice. Newman waited for
him at the foot of the organ staircase to demand an
apology for the invasion of his rights. The answer he
received was, “You did your duty, and I did mine,”
saying which, Braham brushed past him and hurried
away.

right up the stage.
