Here is an extract from a rondo in Luscombe Searelle’s
“ Black Rover ” (played in Australia as ‘ ‘ Isidora ”): ■
My darling Isidora !
Why do I love my darling so,
And long her face to see ?
Perhaps it is because I know
My darling she loves me.
And oh 1 ’tis bliss to feel that she is near,
And comes to me
Here by the sea !
Where I can whisper to my dear.
With nought to fear
And none to hear.”
Great poet, Searelle.
Madame Patti has contracted to give six operatic and si *
concert performances in Russia at 1000 guineas per night.
This is the highest sum she has ever been paid in Europe,
though in South America and the United States she has
frequently received a good deal more.
A member of the New York Shakesperian Society,
laying special stress upon the management and incidents
of the trial scene in “ The Merchant of Venice,” contends
that the author could not possibly have been a lawyer of
the eminence of Francis Bacon, famous as Lord Verulam
was for his exact and encyclopaedic knowledge of legal
technique.

reproaches, the manager replied: “What can I do? He
owns the red waistcoat.”

The Bairnsfather family, now performing at Sydney
Protestant Hall, are not likely' to electrify' the community,
but they give a pleasant, homely entertainment, the sort
of show that attracts and gratifies the non-theatre goer.
The auld mon is a pawkie chield, who sings a comic song
and tells a funny story with considerable humour. Mrs.
Bairnsfather has evidently had much experience on the
concert platform, and Maggie B. has a particularly sweet
and sympathetic voice. Jock and Meg dance gracefully,
and Mr. Nunn fiddles acceptably. Fred. B. Norton
illuminates the front of the house.

Gounod was to get a million of francs for conducting
sixty performances of his works in America. His travel-
ling expenses Were to be paid, and his hotel bills, with
those of his secretary' and servant, and the steamer
fares to cover his journey home as well as out. He was
also to have a share in (he “gate money” when it
exceeded a certain sum. He signed a contract and then
discovered that his health was bad, and that Mrs. Weldon
would probably make a fresh legal attack on him when
she got him on American soil. So he broke it, and now
he is involved in legal troubles independently of the inter-
ference of the litigious soprano.
An ambitious “ super,” whose soul hungered for the
applause he never got, had, on one occasion, a speaking
part of one line : “ The countess awaits you in the con-
servatory, my lord.” Every night he got this off differ-
ently, sometimes he was defiant, anon humble and always
impressive, yet never got a hand. He became desperate
and one evening after getting rid of his message : “ The
countess awaits for you in the conservatory, my lord,” he
inarched right down to the footlights a/id said: “ Let me
also add that the villain who would lay' his hands on a
woman, save by way of kindness, is unworthy the name
of Briton and a man.” He got a big round, likewise the
sack.

“The Diver’s Luck” is an enormously successful drama
this year in New York, the sensation-scene being at the
bottom of the sea. In the first act a millionaire and his
wife are drowned crossing the Atlantic, taking a valuable
box of diamonds with them to the bottom. Later on - the
villain cuts an Atlantic cable, and the hero dives to the
bottom to repair it, while the villain goes to pick up the
diamonds. Sensation with the bodies of the millionaire
and millionairess floating about—fight with an octo-
pus, disc.
“Agitate 1 Agitate !” used to be the cry of an old Mel-
bourne prompter at the Royal, as soon as the drop-curtain
came down. Agitate meant waggling the proscenium
door to lash the audience into frenzy and secure a big call
for the star, who would hang around, loth to depart for
his dressing-room and miss that interesting piece of
guiver—a call before the curtain.

People I Have Met in Sydney.
A “ GUBBANA."
No. I.
DR. Lang, in his “ History of New South Wales,’’
tells an amusing story of old Bungary, the chief
of the Sydney tribe of blacks. The chief had a
great knack of showing how the different
Governors made a bow, but when requested to
make a bow like the then Governor he prudently
refused, saying : “ ’Top, ’top ; baal me do it that
yet; ’top nudda Gubbana come.” Bungary evi-
dently held it sound policy never to exhibit the
reigning “Gubbana.” But in those days “ Gub-
banas ” did reign, for weal or woe, in good earnest,
and stormed, too, very often. Then they dealt
out land, and in rum, so it was as well to keep
friendly with tnem, and Bungary was wise in his
generation.
But we have changed all that now. Instead of
granting us land and selling us rum, they give us
“taffy,” and sell us —well, I hardly know what
they do sell up unless it’s an aide-de-camp or two
now and then for our daughters. The rum was
comforting; as for the A.D.C. —well, let Mrs.
and time tell. I have only met two “ Gub-
banas ” in Sydney. The first, a worn-out diplo-
matist, with the musty flavour of the Foreign
Office clinging to him still —a man bound hand
and foot with red-tape—a round peg in a square
hole; but, de mortals nil nisi bonum, he and his
kind are, I suspect, dead to Australia for ever.
The second, a tired man about town—a man who
reminds me of times gone by, of times which
make one feel inclined tu sing, as did pretty
Nelly Bromley in “ The Creole ” at Toole’s Theatre
(only it wasn’t Toole’s theatre then)
Years do not make me see the clearer,
One feels of age at thirty-nine.
She sang niafy-nine, but it comes to much the
same thing, for now-a-days most of us begin to
feel a bit played-out at thirty-nine. Hereminded
me of times—not so very many years ago, after
all —when Kate and Susie Vaughan were in the
ballet at the Metropolitan Music Hall, long before
Fred. Wellesley made a fool of himself with Kate,
and Maude Branscombe and her sister were ditto
at the Alhambra. Before Florence St. John
deserted the Strand, and Mr. Smith (alias Lith-
Sow James) took to the gentle Marius. When
Jmily Duncan and her sister were still to the
fore, and gave those little parties not a hundred
miles from Winchester-street, Pimlico. Before
St. John’s Wood became so mighty respectable.
When Scott’s was crowded, and the'Globe a howl-
ing wilderness at night. When old Jenkins, the
tobacconist at the corner of the Haymarket
used to make a mistake and fill your glass
with champagne instead of soda, if he knew
you were not particular about your change, when
you stepped in to buy a packet of cigarettes for
pretty Minnie Douglas on your way to 87. Before
Connie Gilchrist came out, or Fail Cairns—
Heaven rest his soul ! —wore tail-coats. When
sprightly Nelly Farren was only about a hundred
years old, but still as young—and good, too —as
evet; and Lord Hopetoun was a red-headed boy
tied to, his mother’s apron-strings up in Linlith-
gowshire. Before ladies vyere allowed downstairs
a,t Eyaus’, and before the poor naughty “ Duke’s”
was shut and the Westminster Aquarium open.
Aes, he reminds me of these things, “ only that
and nothing more.” I don’t remember him in the.
lyorlds of. art, science, literature, diplomacy or
politics. But yet he suddenly burst upon an
astonished world, as full-blown “Gubbana” of
England’s, grandest colony, and all I can say is
“• que djiable aUait-il faire dans rette i/alere ? ”
• * *
The Colonial Office people seem to have in,
their minds just now, with reference to,
■ colonials,” Shylock’s words, “If you tickle
u,s, do we not laugh ? ” So. they tickle us
with a lord, and—we laugh, at least most of us
do. If the “ Gubbana ” can manage to tickle an
earldom for lii,mself out of us, why so much the
better—for him. They tickle us with a “Gub-
bana ” who has shaken hands with so many
people, that op his return to the “ jpother ”
country he may discover that unless h,e alters,
his style —which he will the ultra-refined
will npt shake hands with him. They tickled us
with a “G.ubbana” who had enough money left
of his own to be able to afford to, spend almost
the whole of the inpome he got frojn the colony
in the colony. They tickle us with a “ Gubbana ”■
who brings with him as aides-de-camp, a mini be i;
of young fellows who know yery little of the
World in general, and absolutely nothing of Aus-
tralia in particular. What dreadful mistakes
thpy might make to be sure, in granting the
oppn, sesame to, the kingdom of Government
House ! They might, fog instance, accept
a publican a,ud reject a sinner (in the
sense of having no money). Qpen their arms to,
the flashy daughters of a lady of somewhat lax
ideas on the subject of husbands, and plose the,
doors to quieter girls with purer mothers. Admit
a retired butcher, and exclude a retired officer.
“Pal” with gentlemen whose fathers helped to
make the roads, and overlook gentlemen whose
fathers helped to make history. Shut out the
mistress, and welcome the servant-girl (though
here they might often be acting wisely). Forget
the Oxford man who writes fop a newspaper, and
remember the Potts Point snob who owns it. In
Short, might make such, fppls of themselves
and their master, that all that is decent and wise,
and well-bred in,Sydney, might laugh in itssleeve
and keep aloof. I say they might do any or all of
things, but, perhaps, where ignorance is
bliss ’tis folly to be wise. They tickle us with a
“ Gubbana ’’’who, though lie may be q uite cm fait as
to what goes, on in Pall Mall, is in a state of blissful
ignorance as regards the Pacific. They tickle us
with a negative lord ; let them give us. a, positive
man,. They would fain feed u,s on sugar ; we are
pjd enpugh, to eat strong meat-.
A little tickliner is all very’well, but too much
makes people mad. We aye not all such fools Or
children to bedazzled by a coronet, np.matter how
amiable may be the man who wears it. We don’t
much care what our “Gubbana” wears, on his
head, so long ag he has brains in it. Bah !
lam positively getting serious, and certainly no
one who, knows him would dream of ta- ing those
highly ornamental “Gubbanas” seriously. So,
until the people at home get it fairly into their
heads that we really want someone useful, this
variety will do as well, or perhaps better than,
another. Jolly Nash used in those bygone days
of which I have spoken to sing a song of which'.l
can jvist remember the chorus. I hope New South
Wales, driven mad by too much tickling, will
never- be forced to sing it to Old England. It ran
this way;—
Don’t you be ridiculous ;
Don’t you come and tickle ns ;
Go and tickle someone else ;
You mustn't t ickle me.

The blushing moral policeman in charge
of the Carlton (Vic.) lockup found himself in a
complicated position the other day. He had a
prisoner on hand named Jemima Ann, or some
such name, who was in trouble for profane
language complicated by drunkenness and road-
metal, and he went into her cell to bring her
before the court in the customary way. But when
he opened the door the unprotected female inside
began to shriek, and next moment the officer shot
out again in a confused state of mind, and reported
to the bench that he couldn’t, produce the defen-
dant because she had nothing on. She hadn’t
buried her clothes for there was no place where
she could put them, and she hadn’t burned them,
and if she had shoved them through the window
tliey would be found lying in the yard—anyhow,
the clothes were gone, and nothing but an undis-
guised woman remained. Possibly she had eaten
her entire wardrobe, but the constable refused to
advance an opinion on this point. As it was
obviously impossible to consider the case in the
ordinary way, the prisoner was forcibly put to
bed, and then the magistrate entered the cell and
asked her if she pleaded guilty or not guilty, but
the distracted female returned an evasive answer
and asked him if his grandmother was a monkey,
and then as she started to burst out of bed again

The series of attempts to. set fire to the
Palace Hotel, Melbourne, recall in their malignant
persistency one of the exploits of a noted N.Z. fire-
raiser of 2Q years ago, named Cyrus Haley. He
had been a well-to-do storekeeper, but being bitten
by money-hunger, went headlong into min-
ing speculation. These were the days when the
Thames goldfield was turning out 200,000 ounces
a, month, and when Tom Russell, now C.M.G.
and in London, was the Napoleon of the share-
market. Haley lost all he had in the vortex,
blamed Russell’s manipulations of the market as
the cause, and swore to be revenged,. Russell
had several thousand pounds invested in the N.Z.
Insurance Company and Haley decided to strike
Russell through that corporation. In a very few
months’ time nearly the whole of Queen-street,
Auckland, had been burnt, also the Choral Hall
and a vessel lying at the wharf, and the New
Zealand Insurance was a heavy loser. The place
was in a state of terror till at last’Haley was cap-
tured red-handed and sent up for a long temp
He broke out of Mount Eden Gaol, and before
being recaptured emptied a six-chambered
revolver at Russell through a window of the
latter’s palace at the Pah. Haley was then trans-
ferred tu Dunedin Gaol, from which he made two
escapes, but in the second passed in his checks
with a warder’s bullet through his spine. ‘
That awful accident to some person in
high life, which is always on the verge of taking
place, yet is averted at the last moment, and con-
sequently never comes off, has nearly happened
again without success. There is a dire and soul-
destroying monotony in the inherent cussedness
of that catastrophe which always misses fire, and
the latest incident is as monotonous as the rest.
Governor Hopetoun was attending a cattle-show,
and a cart-horse bolted right at him witli the
intention of pounding him into a squashy mass of
boots and pulp witli its enormous hoofs, and
jumping up and down on his corpse. Any ordinary
man would simply have stepped aside, and let the
animal pass, but the Governor was looking the
other way, and had no idea that death, clad in
hair and hide, was rushing down upon him. Then,
just as the awful tragedy was about to burst on a
horrified people in all its bloodstained naked-
ness, somebody gave his Excellency a shove and
sent him out of the way, and nothing took place.
Also the horse stopped before it reached the place
where Hopetoun had been standing, so that even
if he hadn’t been shoved out of the way there
would have been no incident after all. Later
reports revealed the fact that it was only going at
a slow trot, anyhow, and when the matter was
more fully investigated it was ascertained that it
wasn't there at all. Further, it transpires that the
Governor was at home in bed at the time, and the
only foundation for the rumour lay in the fact
that a Chinaman was nearly drowned in the bar-
hour two weeks previously. His Ex.’s escape,
however, has been the subject of much congratu-
lation, for even though there was no horse
accident it is felt that he might easily have fallen
out of bed and broken his neck, and his life was
only saved by the providential circumstance that
he stayed where he was,

regarded by the po pu .

and deliberate bloodshed
for the purposes of gain,
the very worst and most
diabolical guise in which

people of Australia will
ere long abolish the gal-
lows for ever. At Wagga
to-day a man lies under
sentence of death. “We
are horrified !” cry the
residents—“let him not
be executed in our town.”
At Broken Hill to-day a
man is doomed by law.
‘Take him from our
town, let him not Ire
butchered here,” cry the
people to the authorities.
The people of Wagga, the
people of Broken Hill,
the people everywhere
revolt from the idea of
seeing murder in any
form at their doors, and
cry, “Away with it,
away with it, anywhere ‘
—to the farthest bounds
of the province—to the
farthest limits of our
country !” But it is a
fetich, to which they have
been born and bred—an
old, traditional fetich im-
posed upon them by the
ignorance and brutality
of a long line of rulers
and priests. It will die
with the death of ignor-
ant and brutal statecraft
and priestcraft, even as it
has died in the regener-
ated Italy of Beccaria.
Dear Bulletin, —The leading Australian'
papers have lately had cablegrams that a Bond'
street (London) jeweller had purchased some
500,000 acres of land from the West Australian
Government. You also refer to the matter in
your article on “ Foreign Capital ” (August 23rd).
The cablegram has caused great amusement in the
north-west, as the facts of the case are as follow:
Mr. Streeter, who owns a small pearling-plant
working on the N.W. coast, composed of the
smallest and worst luggers now in these seas, has,
through his manager, purchased the lease of
half-a-million acres of land from the West Aus-
tralian Government, the yearly rental of the whole
of which is £l2 10s., or in reality about as much,
perhaps, as it cost to send the cablegrams an-
nouncing the event. It is a desert waste of sand
and poor scrub, that would require 1000 acres or
more to carry one sheep. It has evidently been
purchased with a view to floating into a public
company. At present it is only used as a
rendezvous for the boats now employed pear-
fishing. If the land is leased the blacks can be
used by the lessee as hired servants , and are
used—that is, any squatter so named can sign--
“save the mark !”—the natives as £ ener ,
servants and make them work in any way '
pleases. There are at present about 100 beau
cattle on this important block, and one
is engaged.— Truth, Roebuck Bay, Weste
Australia.

The London Jews presented a testimonial
to Cardinal Manning, congratulating him on the
jubilee of his priesthood—including his Anglican
service. Jewry is absolutely impartial nowadays.
The Dowager Duchess of Manchester is a
year older than her Hartington, and may soon be
lifted out of her stuffy dowagership to duchess*
dom again, of Devonshire, the present duke
being 82.
Mr. Phonograph Garland began life in the
drapery trade in Auckland. On his tombstone it
may be written that he married a lady who could
write and bought an interest in a machine that
could talk.
Higinbotham’s bust was presented to the
Melbourne Trades-Hall six years ago, Dr. Madden
officiating as spokesman, but the Chief Justice
himself did not attend, and has never been to the
Trades-Hall.

able thing the title must be to the other Doctors
of Law, Cantabs!

The father of the Victorian Bar is now
John Barker, ex-clerk of parliaments, who was
“ called ” in England in 1834 and n Victoria in
1851. Next to him is Sir Archibald Michie, whose
record is, England, 1838, N.S.W., 1839, and Vic-
toria 1852.
That unhappy nobleman, Beerthirst, late
of Victoria, is now addressing envelopes and
copying letters for 30s. a week in a beastly share-
broker’s office. Many an elderly Australian
maiden will shed tears of woe when she reads this
paragraph.

Prince Edward of Saxe-Weimar, needless
to say one of the Guelph horde, who for some
years has been supposed to command the troops in
Ireland, weighs 22-stone. He would be a useful
man in active service, especially when he made up
his mind to fall upon the enemy.
Mr. Justice Williams (Vic.) shed tear*
over a case of manslaughter at the late Sale
Assizes, not over the criminal, but over the victim
—a woman, 84 years of age, who was done to
death by her daughter. After the tears, his
Honor awarded 18 months’ hard.
Bismarck having stated once that: “A
journalist is a man who has failed in his profession
in life,” it is suggested that the ex-Ohancellor’s
epitaph shall be:
He lived a Statesman.
He died a Journalist.
The now celebrated Col. Price organised
his Victorian cavalry in 1885. and they number
1000, which is to be increased to 1200, in two
regiments of 600 each, there being now only one
regiment. Price first made lus mark by organising
mounted police after the Kelly scare.
The report that O’Donovart Rossa had
started to make all honest living in the Whisky
trade is contradicted. He is engaged in trading
off a particularly loathsome brand of cigar, which
smells like Sodom and Gomorrah, and wrecks the
Constitution of the hated Sassenach.
The Prince of Wales was going witli his
gold-bug, banker Hirsch, for a jaunt to the latter's
castle, in Bohemia, but was positively threatened
off it by the Emperors of Germany and Austria,
there being no Jews received at the Vienna Court,
save a pair of Rothsoliildren, in a little-finger
shaking Way.
It is well known that Rossetti buried his
M.B, poems in the coffin at the feet of his wife ;
now it is said that, years after wlieh Rossetti
agreed that the coffin should be Opened for their
recovery, the friends whom he had deputed were
amazed to find “that the wonderful hlonde hair
had grown to her feet, ttnd formed a perfect net-
work of gold over the papers.”
The Age is of opinion that the qualities
which go to make a Wellington would probably
unfit a man to become a Chief Justice Higin-
botham. That’s true. The born soldier isn't a
born socialist by long chalks. In fact, on all
subjects except scientific slaughter the born
soldier is a good deal of a born fool. Caesar and
Napoleon to the contrary notwithstanding.
Old Moltke has been recently congratu-
lated on turning the corner of DO. Turenne,
killed at 04, was thought a worn-out veteran at
the age when Moltke began to creep into notice,
two years before Sadowa, and six before Sedan#
Metz and Paris. Radetsky, at 83, fought his
amazingly active Novara campaign of 1849.
Havelock got no show till 62, and Clyde was 65 in
the Indian Mutiny, his first action being
Vimiera, at 16 years old.
Mr. John Williams, the N.S.W. Crown
solicitor, who began practice as an attorney in
1842, has gone for a few months’ leave, and will
be temporarily succeeded by the astute Mr, Robert
Smith. When it was suggested that a N.S.W.
Supreme Court Judge should be sought for
amongst the attorneys, the Bar article being
scarce, Mr. John Williams’ name was bracketed
with those of the late Messrs. Stephen Campbell
Brown and E. A. Scarvell.
James Munro, the new Victorian Premier,
was born in 1832, and is thus 58. He started
Victoria Permanent Building Society in 1865, on
the model of the Edinburgh Scottish Property
Investment Co., and poor little Jemmy Mirams
was tempted to pot against kettle. At a Carlton
election Munro’s opponent bought up all the meet-
ing-places, and, therefore, teetotal Munro ran up
a new hall for himself, at a cost of £2OOO, because
he wouldn’t hold a meeting in a hotel.
Judge Boucaut (S.A.), the broad and
comprehensive policy man, comes into a nice not
of money by the decease of old McCulloch, whose
daughter he married, Boucaut is one of the most
fearless and unconventional of men, and has been
known to take off his boots and stroll down the
jetty at Glenelg to his yacht on a Sunday, right
through the church people. After delivering a
judgment in the Supreme Court on an intricate
case, he will go dowh to the bay, take off his
boots, and help » fisherman off the beach with his
boat.

appears the last of the Governments which form-
ally approved of the Parkes scheme of “ Federation
under the Crown.”
“Parliament is largely what the reporters
make it,” says Chief Secretary Deakin. Then,
again, the reporters are exactly what their pro-
prietors (this term to be taken literally) make
them.
When the public gallery of the New South
Wales Assembly Chamber, which holds 120 people,
was opened to the public on the evening of the
no-confidence debate, 56 persons were found com-
fortably seated therein.
Chief Secretary Deakin is regularly played
out just now and has made a dying-swan oration,
snivelling over Gillies. Alfred had better take a
turn at table-turning, now they are turned. What
can he do ? Stick to Dunky ?
The Ballarat Trades and Labour Council,
anxious to be represented on the magisterial
bench, have submitted to the Minister for Justice
for appointment four names: one a mining engine-
driver ; another, a bricklayer; a third, a tailor :
and the fourth, a blacksmith.
Coalition rules both sides of the Victorian
Parliament now, that is to say, the Tories boss
both the Ministry and Opposition. It is laugh-
able to hear talk about the Labor Party having
extinguished Gillies. Victoria Permanent and
Federal Bank Munro the Labor Party !

difference between true and spurious friendship.”
It doesn't take long for a colleague of Parkes to
become fully aware of that interesting fact.

John Burns asserts that he has nevef
advised strikes, that they furnish a crude, suicidal
and brutal method of settling a dispute. Strikes
dislocate and demoralise trade, and he would do
away with them altogether. Hence he wishes to
transfer the conflict to the House of Commons and
to the ballot-boxes in the constituencies.
Gillies was Victorian Premier five years,
but M'Culloch had the longest continuous turn, 60
days more than Gillies, during the reign of that
great combination which, at one time or other,
included Higinbotham, Michie, Fellows (a month
or two), Heales (till he died), Francis, Verdon, and
Grant - who replaced Heales as Land Minister.
In the Victorian want of confidence
division the Government was beaten on a fairly-
called division by 55 votes to 35. In the N.S.W.
want of confidence motion the . Opposition was
defeated on a surprise division by 46 to 23. In
all such cases, divisions should be taken at an
agreed time in the interests of honesty and
finality.
Switzerland simmers in the same throes as
have been passed through by the American United
States, struggle between the Central Government
and local State Independence, but with this differ-
ence ; that in America the Democrats had a
reasonable fear of Csesarism, whereas the Swiss
Liberals rule centrally, and are resolved to rule
locally, all round.
The prospective attempt on the part of the
associated employers to bring to their knees, by
means of imported labour and starvation, the
370,000 members of the British marine unions, is
simply a mad scheme to incite an enormous body
of sturdy and resolute men, shut out by monopoly
from the sources of- production, to the use of
dynamite and the fire-stick.
After all, where’s the benefit in swapping
Premier Dunky Gillies for Premier Jimmy Munro?
Gillies is a Liberal hitched and harnessed on to
the Tories, like Pitt, while Munro is a solid,
bound - hand - and - foot -to - capital Conservative,
hitched on to the Liberal chariot. Never was
Liberal—look at his face, a building society
Mookbeeth, with hacked claymore.
The effete Constitution of Western Aus-
tralia is about to go down to its grave unwept,
nnhonour’d and unsung,” except by a small Tory
band. Its last dying kick, in the shape of
electoral conditions which have secured the disen-
franchisement of one-fourth o| the country, has
incited the incensed people to anake “Manhood
Suffrage ” the slogan of the first general election
under Responsible Government.
Coppde, the academician and poet, is a
sentimental socialist. At the brilliant entertain-
ments to which he is invited he is apt,
abstractedly, to inquire from the guests : : “If
they know their luxury is so many miseries ? Do
they ever think of it ? Do they think of it as often
as they should ? Do they think of it all?” It was
Coppee’s question which vwas asked in the
“objectionable ” leaflet circulated by the N.S.W.
Labour Defence Committee among the guests at
the Carington banquet the other night.
There is a remarkable paper in London
Spectator, to hand by last mail, on “Colonial
Loyalty and the Vatican.” The significant thing
in the article is a thorough recognition that the
Australian Republic, or, as the Spectator
calls it Democratic Federation, is inevitable.
Otherwise the trend is that Catholicism, as a
democratic and republican power, will gain
ground in Australia, as in the United States.
The writer points out that loyal priests are at a
discount. The article is decidedly one to be read.
Says Michael Davitt of the policy of his
new paper, The Labour World ;
The overthrow of landlordism will be one of its most
cherished ambitions. “The Land for the People,” and
not for any class, will be our watchword, and to this
battle-cry we will try and rally the democratic forces of
the three countries. The public ownership of land
mines, minerals, royalties, woods, forests, fisheries, sea-
shore rights and railways will be strenuously advocated.
The revenues derived from these sources must be devoted
to the good of the industrial community if we are to
battle successfully against the poverty and wretchedness
which class legislation and individualistic greed have
bred in the organisation of society.
Lady Dilke says the arguments of the
Capitalists and most of the clergy remind her of
“ a fond mamma who had two children—Tommy
and Maria. When mamma had a beautiful cake
or fruit, she used to call Tommy and say : ‘ Now’
Tommy, divide that with Maria in a Christian
spirit.’ That meant that he was to give Maria
the bigger half of the cake or the apple. Tommy
was a good boy, a very good boy, and he went on
bearing it for a long time, but at last he said :
‘ Mamma, mayn’t Maria begin to divide the cakes
now in a Christian spirit ?’ ” Capital has played
the Maria with Tommy the worker long enough,
and it is now Maria’s turn to give Tommy the
biggest hunk.
A person named Chapman has started to
deliver a course of lectures throughout Australia
on some hazy object or fact or animal which
threatens to undermine what he calls the “Pro-
testant succession” to the British throne. The
old Orange fanatic can, like Parson Madgwick
Griffith and Co., never understand that the
world has moved on a little since the siege of
Derry. There will be no British throne at all
soon, and no “ succession, ” and as the only duty
of the sovereign is to draw an enormous screw and
hunt up pensions for her sisters, and her cousins,
and her uncles, it doesn’t matter whether she is a
Protestant or a Mohammedan, or a cow-and-cat
worshipper, or an individual who attends Joe
Somes’ tabernacle. The matter isn’t of the
slightest importance, anyway.

not sent on their way rejoicing as was Baron

In 1827, the Sydney Turf Club gave a dinner at
which certain disparaging remarks ahd unfavour-
able comparisons were made between Darling and
his predecessor, the band playing not “Over the
water to Charley” but “Over the hills and far
away,” when the Governor’s health Was proposed.
On leaving the colony no complimentary banquet
was tendered, but H.E. ’s opponents, lev! by W. C.
Wentworth, gave a free banquet at Yaucluse
where a bullock was roasted whole, and drink
distributed ad lib. As the ship Hooghly, with
Darling aboard, passed Yaucluse the assemblage
hooted and hissed most energetically.

Australian Governors’ wives should, re-
member the Bowen incident. The estimable lady
of that bucolic Governor went ’Ome for a trip and
on her return—well, “absence makes the heart
grow fonder” sometimes. And sometimes it
doesn’t.
* * *
“An accident of birth has made your
Governor Lord Carington,” said Gov. Hopetoun
at the big Carington banquet, at Sydney. But
should not the statement be reversed, thus : —“ An
accident of birth has made Lord Carington your
Governor?” How, for example, would this sound,
“An accident of birth has made Victoria’s
Governor Lord Hopetoun ?” Would young Hope-
toun really have the impudence' to stand up and in
snoh words convey the idea that he would have been
Governor of Victoria perforce of his own innate
ability, apart altogether from his inherited titles
and riches ? It is amusing to see how Governor
No 1 (you see we don’t put a bill-point after the ‘o’)
sticks to Governor No. 2.
* * *
Captain Helbert, in his speech at the Car-
ington farewell banquet, said he felt the greatest
diffidence in responding on behalf of the fair sex,
in that he was the only remaining member of
H.E.’s staff who had not had the good fortune to
be able to avail himself of the fair chances which
they saw in such profusion around them every day
and that night above them. (Laughter and “Oh !
oh !”) The gallant captain is widely credited with
being engaged to N.S. W. richest heiress, but he
evidently appreciates the proverb about there
being many a slip ’twixt the cup and the lip.
# # #
The S.M. Herald publishes a long article
which, after you pan out of it all about the
turquoise-blue sea, and the wooded walks and
grassy lawns of Government House, Sydney, and
the softly-breathed syllables and whispered vows
of youths and maidens, means that Lady
Carington has been a magnificent success as
a match-maker, and that a lot of the local
aristocracy will hereafter “link their happiness
with the thoughts of a noble name and join their
first appreciation of each other’s charms with the
opportunities lent them by a kindly and a willing
hostess.” Smooth, easy prophecy ! The N.S. W.
Greased Lightning Divorce Bill is now pretty well
through.
* - * *
Old Sir Alfred Stephen, when sworn in as
Acting Governor, of N.S.W., had thirteen guns
fired in his honor. As this expensive old gentle-
man enjoys a huge pension, it is surely rather too
much that the public should spend a lot of money
in senseless thunder-blasting to emphasize the fact
that he is reaching out his bediamonded fore-finger
for more perks.
* # #
The Perth (W.A.) Liedertafel have duly
serenaded Governor “Bill” Robinson, and that
troubadour enchanting is alleged to be preparing
a new part-song for his admirers—a song about
moons, and maidens, and lingering embraces, and
suchlike giddiness.
* * *
Baron Carington evidently rather fancies
the peroration of his citizens’ banquet speech, as
he has just shouted it verbatim into the phono-
graph. It runs: “Generous is the nation which
can thus speed (evidently a misprint for ‘feed ’)
its parting guests, and fortunate those guests who
found their welcome at once in adoption, and
whose farewell ‘ leaves half their hearts behind.’ ”
Was it “ hearts ” your Lordship said? Who-oop!

* * *
A prisoner With a moving tale to relate
appeared last week in a Victorian police-court.
He said that he and the policeman who ran him
in were in love with the same girl, but the maiden
turned up her nose at the officer and refused to
smile upon his suit. Then, by way of vengeance,
the debased “cop” hit his rival over the head with
his official club and arrested him on a charge of
assault, his object being to hug the lady at the
back door while the other fellow was in gaol. It
was a mean, low, beastly scheme, but sin generally
prospers in this world, and so the defendant was
locked up. And now in the visions of his dreams
upon his plank bed he sees a leering constable

in a cold perspiration and asks where the thunders

* * *
The very latest gubernatorial puff of the
new people who are about to move into
Government House, Sydney, is that Lady Jersey
“has a true woman’s heart.” We are now
anxiously awaiting advices touching her temper,
tongue, and other peculiarly womanly charac-
teristics.
* * *
The caretaker of the grounds of Melbourne
Gun Club has just been fined for cruelty to a
number of pigeons. This reminds us that certain
members and hangers-on of Sydney Gun Club
have not been fined, but have profited exceedingly
by their cruelty to a number of once gorgeous
“pigeons,” who have now not a feather to fly
with. Anyone who would hear an eloquent ser-
mon on the folly of backing the gun should learn
the life-histories up to date of certain “ flash ”
young monied Sydney fools who fancied they could
make money out of the Hebrews.
# * #
What is this noise we hear like the crack-
of a cocoa-nut? That noise, dear friends, is made
by the skull of a “ free ’’-labourer landing
“ whoosh !” on the pavement. He has just been
fired out by his grateful employer who, now that
he has done with him, pointed out that the so-called
two years’ engagement certain was all make-
believe.
* * *
Spagnoletti: “I object, on principle, to
Hospital Sunday. It is mainly a church-adver-
tisement, never thought of till the church was on
the down grade. Half the population don’t go to
church, so the scheme is half-and-half. Down
with charity altogether, supply it by taxation,
equally, and let’s have done with snuffle, Most
people give nothing, and there are those who give
more than they ought.”

“ Justitia ” (evidently a woman) Wants to
know how it is that so many men, who have
deserted and do not contribute to the support of
their wives and families, are retained in the
N.S. W. Government service ? The question is a
pertinent one.
* * *
There is serimlS trouble in Melbourne
between the Australian Natives’ Association
(male) and the Austral Salon (female). The Salon
is a shadowy and indefinite body with no par-
ticular object, and it is about as hard to fix as a
baseless assertion at the bottom of a pit; and the
Natives’ Association is a gathering without any
Visible purpose, which may be briefly described as
a sort of insane Moses after the light had gone
out. The Salon, however, has gone out of its way
to state that the average Native is an ignorant in-
dividual, blown up with his own conceit, and
vacant in his intellect, and it also hints that he is
lanky, vulgar, and incapable of any emotion, and
that he spends most of his time in passing resolu-
tions about his own greatness. Also, the Natives,
in their turn, are of opinion that the Salon is as
stupid as a shovelful of mud, and doesn’t know
enough to walk round a tree and find its way
homo again. As it is ungentlemanly to contradict
anybody, The Bulletin cheerfully accepts the
assertions on both sides.
# * *
Some lady cricketers seem to be training
down Richmond (Melb.) way, but the present
“form” of the angels is far from brilliant. The
bowling, as a rule, is about two yards off the wicket,
and if perchance a golden haired Spofforth manages
to put in a straight one, it is a case of “skirts
before” with the dimpled Murdoch, who never can
hit them when they come like that. A lack of
discipline is very apparent too, and the game
usually goes bung because everybody wants three
innings at once, or because they all want to go out
and field close to the solitary young man who has
wandered up to see the fun.
* # *
There is now an awful puzzle in Melbourne
over street-numbers. They have re-numbered the
city, but people are afraid to take off the old
numbers with which their businesses have been
long associated. Consequently nearly every place
has two numbers, widely different, and a source
of endless confusion.

* * #
One of the most successful entertainments
of the year was given lately at a Victorian coup,
try town by the local branch of the A. N. A. The
branch being hard-upj it was resolved that every
lady should be required to pay for her ticket, and
as none of the ladies thought the Natives Worth
paying fr r there was a serious scarcity. One of
the Ideal barmaids turned up, for an enthusiastic
member had bought a ticket and given it to the
pretty girl at the hotel, and she had passed it on
to the ugly, red-headed girl with the bad temper
and the latter attended punctually. When she
realized the situation she called the principal
Native a thief and a monkey, and retired in dis-
gust. Then the members proceeded to waltz
wearily with each other, arid professed to be full
of hilarious mirth, but eighteen of them sneaked
off in the first half hour, and two had a fight, and
six got drunk, and at half-past nine the company
went home the back way and escaped into bed.
# # #
Now that the list of persons assisted by
the N.S. W. Relief Society during the late floods has
been published, it is openly stated that one of the
recipients drove to the local township in his buggy
to place his share of the spoil in the bank. This
man turns in an honest penny by letting monies
out at interest, Another recipient is the well-
known owner of some 40 head of horse-stock,
many of them being high-priced draught mares!
* # #
The latest pretty thing in feminine attire
is a green silk stocking with a serpent worked on it
in gold. The offensive beast is coiled round the
stocking, and his head is visible just above the
boot (which is at variance with the fact that snakes
descend trees tail-first). These circumstances are
presented without any attempt at comment. Our
soul is too full for utterance.

B. 0. Rodd (admitted in 1833), and Mr. Henry
Burton Bradley being still very much in evidence.

In a Melbourne divorce-suit the usual
order of things is reversed. The petitioner-
husband —complains of cruelty, inasmuch as his
wife disciplined him with the poker, tongs and
carving-knife.

“Let him marry the gal! ” is the familiar
magisterial solution for an affiliation case, but a
Melbourne girl lately surprised their Worships
amazingly. Bringing up a fellow for the support
of his infant son, she blankly refused his offer of
marriage, because she could not love a man who
had deceived her. This was quite out of the
depth of beakdom. She could give herself away
for love, but she could not sell herself for Respect-
ability.

Down in the Ballarat district a pair of
ardent lovers have tangled themselves up in a
religious difficulty which seems likely to terminate
fatally. He was a Roman Catholic, whilst she
was still clinging tight (spiritually speaking) to
John Wesley on the day when first they met.
They kept on meeting until the position of affairs
became harrowing to both parties, and they used
to weep over their diversity of faith and tear their
hair together by the light of the pale moon. It

was after one particularly humid leavetaking that
each of the lovers decided in secret to take up the
other’s religion and so remove the awful barrier
that had hitherto separated soul from soul. They
accordingly swapped beliefs and creeds unbe-
known to anybody, and then they wrote letters
desiring one another to meet at the old, old place
and come for a long, long walk, because they bad
something very particular to say. At the usual bliss-
ful hour they flew down to the trysting-place on the
wings of anticipation and exchanged hugs and
sweet mysterious glances on the usual liberal scale
until the maiden began to explain how she had
gone to the poll early that morning and plumped
for the Pope, whereupon the boy of her heart fell
upon the cold ground artel swote by John Wesley
that he wished he was dead, he did, because two
short oily brethren had snuffled him into their fold
on the previous everting- The fact that the old
barrier separates the young people worse than ever,
has laid then! both on beds of sickness, where they
will probably fade away like flowers unless the
spirit moves them to arise and flee to Atheism as
being the last refuge left them in the time of
trouble*
Herbert Schmalz’s fine picture, “ Too
Late,” which was the popular favourite in the
Grosvenor Gallery Exhibition, at Melbourne, has
been purchased for Bendigo Art Gallery! Seven
hundred guineas seems a reasonable price for such
an uncommonly effective work*
* * #
A notice posted up in a certain Melbourne
bank forbids the clerks to smoke in the streets of
the city during luncheon-hour! This looks like a
delicate endeavour to push trade for the nearest
pub. whither the young aristocrats at £75 a year
will naturally fly for a furtive puff.
# * #
The Melbourne police have just, by some
unaccountable accident, stumbled across the
possibility that a man who commits acts of pur-
poseless indecency in public may be a little wrong
in his head. The fact that such fellows are at the
very least monomaniacs has been sticking out for
centuries, but the law goes on sending the poor
wretches to the very place where their brain-
trouble is bound to become incurable,

# # *
“ Pendragon ” gives the Heir-Apparent a
decided jar re His Highness’s alleged acceptance
of “benefactions” from that defunct toady, Sir
James Mackenzie, Speaking of the spurious
Mackenzie (who bought the name along with
certain Scotch estates) and the sneers that have
only appeared in print since his death, “Pen-
dragon ” says :—“ So far as his buying his way to
the smiles of a certain personage goes, it is, to my
thinking, far worse for a leader of fashion to
forget the obligations of his rank and position,
and become a pensioner and a parasite where he
ought to be a prince and a patron, than it is for
the successful trader or other parvenu who has
bis social ambitions to follow the shortest road to
their gratification.” Well, to The Bulletin’s
thinking, there’s nothing to choose between them.
What are the obligations of royal rank, judged by
the experience of ages past ? When was the
position of any prince other than the position of
a sponger ?
A member of the Church of England
writes to a Sydney evening paper complaining
that a suburban clergyman, recently discovering
that two of his class of intending communicants
were domestic servants, separated them from the
others!
• * *
A story comes to us of a lot of drunken
squatters who met at the principal local pub. to
give one of their number a ‘ ‘ send-off, ” at a late
hour were filling the night air with shrieks,
obscene jokes, drunken songs and yells of “Fill
’em up again,” when they became suddenly aware
that in the next apartment, from vyhich they were
only separated by a thin partition, was the
governess of a big neighbouring squatter, and who,
robbed of her slumbers and knowing them all
personally, could not fail to put down each witti-
cism (?) to its utterer. It suddenly became
“Tiinsh go bedsh.”

vasser and collector for a large mercantile house.
In the course of his duties he knocked at the door
of a dwelling in an Eastern suburb, and a lady
appeared who intimated that she was just then
dining. The canvasser passed on, but when some

The station-sergeant, having brains, re-
fused to take the charge, and told this idiotic
representative of law and order to return home
and sober-up.

torian Permanent Artillery last week had to fix
up a lot of cannon trophies at Melb. Town Hall,
for the-swagger Naval and Military ball, open
only to members of the Defence Forces, and were
then insulted with the offer of one shilling apiece!
How on earth is it that while Price has compara-
lively democratised the cavalry, the infantry and
artillery are such compost - heaps of snobbiculi
with the safeguard of a £3O uniform ?

Within a few feet on one side of the splen-
did Sydney Centennial Hall is being raised an

Rev. Fraser was on the nail at the Sydney
Congregational Synod last week, in pointing out
that the workman created ten times as much
capital as he used to. For whom? Even the poor
parson doesn’t get his fair. Whack -the ballet gets
more of the results of Labour’s sweat.
The appointment of Mr. “ Teddy” Bramble
to the Telegraph-Inspectorship of the Northern
District of N. S. W., appears to be a good one.
“ Teddy” has no friends in high places, and hasn't
influence enough to secure the release of a vagrant
goat from the pound, but he is one of the very
best scientific electricians in the Australian public
service, and as a comic singer is second to nobody
in Sydney. The Bulletin is asking itself which
did the trick with the Minister—the singing or
the science?
Says the Queen of Roumania (Carmen
Sylva): “It is better to .confess to a physician
than to a priest. You tell the priest that you
detest mankind ; he replies that you are not a
Christian! The physician gives you a dose of
rhubarb, and thereupon you love your fellow-
creature.” Bah! Old Coroner Youl put it far
better twenty years ago : “ Many a man has been
plucked from the arms of Beelzebub and placed in
the bosom of Abraham by a blue-pill and a black-
draught.”
Marcus Clarke use 1 to tell an amusing
yarn about his friend, Clayton, the attorney, long
since departed. Clayton was hard as nails—a
regular marble or mahogany man—and ascribed it
to his practice of taking morning sun-baths i.c,,
perambulating, Adamite fashion, about a Garden
of Eden which he possessed beyond Oakleigh. He
persuaded Marcus to take a sun-bath, but they
were pulled up by hearing a ripple of silvery
laughter frqm-behind the bushes. Eves were out,
in full Parisian costume, of course, and narrowly
missed seeing the sun-bathers, wherefore Marcus
declared he wouldn’t sun-bathe nearer Melbourne
than Bullarook Forest.
Duncan Gillies, when Minister for Rail-
ways in the old anie-and-unti-Bevry Government,
had a penchant for champagne. He was affable in
those times, and used to “ include the reporter ”
singular number only—the Argus man. But
then every reporter who turned up used to say he
represented the Argus. If an Age man, con-
fessing himself such, showed his nose, it was—
“No, nothing to-day,” gruffly. But with Berry,
came Woods at the Railways, and exit Gillies,
Then arrived the Age men’s revenge. Woods,

example before the eyes of Unionists the body of
the unhappy tnan Sandon, for ten years a Wqol-
scouter with one firm, who went out oh strike
and afterwards failed to get employment. Sandon
Wasn’t “a strong man ” —was evidently a very
weak one. United Labour does not intend to
Commit suicide.
Dear Bulletin* —At Yetman police-court
(N.S.W.) on 21st Oct. 35 shearers —of which the
undersigned was one—were charged before Mr;
Lawson, P.M., and Messrs., W. Croaker and
Adams, Js.P., with unlawfully refusing to worki
Wo were fined £5 each, levy and distress, costs
4s 3d, and 3s professional costs ; in default 14
days. As one of the defendants expressed his in-
tention of serving the 14 days (or, as he put it,
“taking it out ”), Mr. Lawson stated that had he
been aware that any man had any intention of
“ taking it out ” he would have forfeited the whole
of the wages of all the men, and, he continued,
“if any of your number will not pay the fine, I
will do so nowand taking his watch from his
pocket and laying it on the table before him, said i
“1 now give you five minutes to decide, and
should you carry out your intentions I will now
forfeit the wages of every man.” The wages due
amounted to from £2O to £4O. When one of the
delegates gave notice of appeal theP.M. raised his
hand above his head and said (bringing his hand
flat on the table), “then plank down your
money.” Then the delegate planked it down
with a rap.— George Hawkins.
Is it Suicide?
Mark Twain tells the story : that at the inquest
held on the body of Buck Fanshaw- —who during
the delirium of a raging typhoid fever had taken
arsenic, shot himself through the body, cut his
throat, and jumped out of a four-storey window,
breaking his neck —the jury, after due deliberation,
sad and tearful, but with intelligence unblinded
by its sorrow, brought in a verdict of “ death by
the visitation of God.”
Buck undoubtedly committed suicide, and so
does every one who neglects the first symptoms of
approaching sickness and disease. Nature always
gives ample notice of any disturbance of physical
processes ; sometimes it is neuralgia, sometimes a
sharp shooting pain in the abdomen or side, or a
dull or throbbing headache ; no two persons get
the same symptoms. What you have to do is to
attack the first symptoms, for if consumption,
dropsy, cancer or Bright’s disease once gets a start,
you cannot stop its headlong course to the grave.
These are facts there is no disputing, for all the
medical skill in the world cannot do much for you
when real organic disease has set in.

Adelaide was the first Australian city to
have a Municipal Corporation, the Act being
passed in 1840. Sydney and Melbourne followed
in 1842. 1800 is consequently the jubilee of
municipal institutions in Australasia.
The German infantry are not allowed sucks
now, it being considered that greasing the feet is
more economical, and better for marching pur-
poses. The Frenchwoman who railed at their
want of handkerchiefs has another jibe now.
A London revolutionary paper is entitled
Chattekton’s Commune, and has the sub-title of
“ The Atheistic Communistic Scorcher. ” It is a
single sheet, badly printed, and the editor
scribes himself as “The Uneducated Editor,
Parish-damned Compositor, and Penniless-pro-
prietor,” and says, “ We write our own articles,
set our own type, and, having no press, rub every
letter up with our own five fingers.” Poor enth-
stast; the seed of future of revolutions.
Au enterprising Melbourne firm has
opened up a glorious vista of possibilities for the
“ tea-gift ” business. Amongst a thousand house-
hold necessaries offered for selection by purchasers
of a pound of eighteenpenny tea is Bellamy’s
“ Looking Backward.” The book is growing in
demand, too. Women are taking it in preference
to the elegant but uninstructive breakfast cruet,
or the bedroom flower-vase, and the busy districts
of Prahran and Collingwood are swallowing
socialism with their Souchong.
Dear Bulletin. —Apropos to your cartoon
in this week’s issue, “A Porter’s Romance,” in
which a “giggling country maiden” mistakes a
Coffee Palace attendant for a Naval officer, audi
alteram partem. I, when a boy, and wearing the
livery of “ Her Most Gracious” as a youngster in
the service, have upon numerous occasions been
ordered (under the impression that I was a railway
porter) by intending passengers, to open the train
doors, afford time-table information, etc,, at such
times as I was fool enough to appear in an inland
town otherwise than in plainclothes. Yours, etc,,
Wooden Eeigate.

MELBOURNE CUP.
Hon. D. S. Wallace’s Carbine, lOst. 51b. (Ramage) .. 1
Mr. E. E. Galley’s bg Highborn, fist. 81b. (Egan) .. 2
Mr. J. M’Langhlin’s ch c Oorreze, 7st. 31b. (Moran) .. 3
There were thirty-nine starters, and Carbine won by three
lengths from Highborn, Oorreze being a length' away,
third, in 3min. 28Jsecs., a quarter-second faster than
Dunlop’s time in ’B7, hitherto the fastest on record. The
only thing that temporarily . reconciles an honest man to
the rotten state of racing matters, is the victory of a
heavily-weighted public favourite like Carbine.
# # *
The glories of vice-regality were decidedly off
colour at Flemington on Derby Day, but the sun shone
brightly all the same upon a large and fashionable Lawn
muster. Perhaps so many well-dressed and well-looking
women have never before shown up for the Derby display,
or perhaps it was that a slight reduction in the general
quantity brought the quality into greater prominence.
Anyhow, there was a dress rehearsal that promised a first
class show at the Cup, whereat the Oaringtons would
make their appearance and contribute a little necessary
“tong” to the Govingment ’Ouse party. Only in the
vice-regal box could the effects of the strike be detected
outside that sacred structure things wore an usually
festive aspect. A rise in the price of coal makes no odds
on a hot Derby Day, and if the people who usually punt
were content to let their “fancies” run loose because
business had been slack—well, that fact alone would
account for a considerable percentage of cheerfulness.
Even the successful backer is less jubilant than the man
who meant to have his pound on a loser but played for
safety at the last moment. It was wrong to say that the
Strike had made no difference to Flemington. It kept
hundreds from dropping their money quite as freely as of
yore.
# * #
The Admiral’s win was a triumph for public
form over public rumour. On paper-as The Bulletin
stated when discussing the Caulfield Stakes - nothing
seemed likely to beat Mr. Cook’s horse. Anon the
“ books ” betrayed a sinister anxiety to lay against the
apparent good thing, and so late as last Wednesday some
knowing individuals took odds that neither Titan, Gibral-
tar, nor The Admiral would start for the Derby. Whilst
the one favourite lost friends, the other—Annesley—gained
them, The Bulletin being of their number. The Ballarat
money came pouring in for the Neckersgat colt, until lie
threatened to start at nothing longer than 0 to 4. Then
The Admiral became very “ right,” and the people who
surmised that he was “wrong” had to eat their sus-
picions and seek comfort in the thought that he wasn’t
certain to beat the other horse, anyway. The public
favoured Annesley on the day, a circumstance which
shows how faith in Hales’ mounts has weakened since the
death of Mr. White. Had The Admiral been running in
the good old colours he would have gone to the post
even money chance.
* * *
After the event everybody knew that nothing
could beat the winner, yet many who saw the horse
•addled were disposed to think him less blooming, just
as they thought Annesley was more blooming, than on
the Guineas day, three weeks before. The latter, though,
never had his nose in front once during a slow run race,
and the cry that “ Neckersgats don’t stay” will not
explain his bad performance. Probably, Annesley is but
a moderate animal, and equally probably (this being
written before the Cup throws more light upon the
matter) the three-year-olds are moderate all round. The
ridiculous ease with which Carbine walked away from
Qresford and Loyalstone in the Melbourne Stakes, made
The Admiral’s victory at Caulfield seem smaller than it
looked at the time.
# # #
Whatever may have been the Derby field’s
“class” it is tolerably certain that the youngsters
saddled up for the Maribyrnong Plate were not brilliant.
None of them had been very highly tried, and public
opinion upon the whole crowd was indicated by the
betting-market. Harrington and Ascot, who had both
been beaten by the very mean-looking Submission at
Caulfield, were made favourites, merely because the
others—barring Yarran—had scarcely any followers.
Hales upon Corvette—wearing the arm-band usually asso-
ciated with a “second string”—was allowed to go out at
10 to 1, and nearly landed the filly (another Caulfield dis-
appointment) a winner. Betting' on a Maribyrnong Plate
has not been so light and languid for years past.
* * #
Chaldean walked around the paddock for an
hour or two on Derby Day, and, judging from what could
be seen.of him in his clothes, the horse would peel in
good condition for Tuesday’s race. Chintz looked very
so-so when stripped for the Stakes, and Melos was
generally thought to carry less muscle than he did a year
ago. Singapore moved more easily than usual, and may,
it the weather keeps warm, win a good race before the end
of the week.
* # .'#
Mr, “Titan” Brodribb pulled off some heavy
wagers on Saturday, and was understood to be a few
thousands in front at the end of the agony.
# * *
The Melbourne Cup is a noble institution for
the fatted bookmaker, but for the plentiful idiot
who backs the favourite and perpetually loses, and yet
returns to back another favourite next year, it. amounts
to very little. The whole history of the ( up, from its first
beginning nearly 30 years ago, is one long series of evi-
dences concerning the rampant insanity of trying to pick
a winner. The public commenced its fatuous career in
1861 by laying its shirt on Mormon, and when Archer
came in it went aVay cleaned out and disgusted. Next
year it piled its money on Archer, and was successful, but
as the odds were only 2 to 1 the profits were very humble,
while in 1863 all Australia went in and staked everything
down to the kitchen poker on the chances of Denmark and
Musidora, and when they were both left the nation
departed for home naked. Roebuck was the dead bird in
1864, and he went where the deceased fowl is always
going, and everybody was let in. Toryboy, who won in 1865,
was offered at 100 to 1 without takers, and the two animals
which carried all the money might as well have been head
so far aa their supporters were concerned. Next year a
long-suffering community bet Us immortal soul on Falcon,
and took the short road to the' Benevolent Asylum as
usual. Twelve months later four horses started on almost
equajly short odds, and by a special dispensation of
Providence one of them came in a winner, whereby the
shorn lamb didn’t lose much more than half its
The United States is a nation of traders. Two-
thirds of the business men of the Republic use
Estkrbrook’s ‘Falcon” Pen. Samples free at
nil Stationers.
money. People learned wisdom by experience this lime,
and in 1868 they bet all they could raise on the winner of
It would have been all right if he had won, but he
didn’t, and Glencoe, who wiped him out, carried hardly
any money at all. Circassian, in 1869, was the surest
“rrioral” ever known in Australia, but when a small
kingdom was staked on him at 2 to 1 lie went to pieces,
Warrior, a low, mean, underhand 15 to 1 beast came
in first. country supported him and whooped
for him in 1870, and he promptly caved in to ihake way for
an unknown animal at 12 to 1 that nobody had ever heard
of. Pearl won in 1871 J and anybody could have got 100
to 2 about him. the day before the race, while two animals
that vyere backed for their weight in bank-notes perished
miserably. The favourite ran nearly last in 1872, while a
10 to 1 brute lumbered in victorious amid general curses
and execrations. The backers got a show in 1873, but in
1874 the crowd plunged madly at 2 to 1, and then a dis-
guised cqw, which carried not one cent upon itsheglectcd
back, took the stakes- People cursed a good deal, and
laid everything they had on Imperial next year at 5 to 2,
and another animal which was supposed to be only fit for
the catsmeat industry left the expensive horse far behind,
"he second favourite won in 1876, and the favourite—
j-hester—in 1877 ; and then, in 1878, the money was laid
m a heap on Melita, and when the despised Caiamia
reached the post the public was carried home on a shutter.
Lhe accumulated wealth was stacked on Sweetmeat and
Le Loup at the next Cup, but nobody thought of Darri-
well. ‘*nd everybody was left. Things improved a very
little in 1880, and then, in 1881, Darebin was the dead
hud of the year, and being a good deal too dead, Zulu
took the pool. Any odds could have been obtained about
him, but then nobody wanted odds on Zulu until
it was too late. The observations used this time were
a little worse even than usual, and the English language
broke down miserably for want of adequate blasphemy.
Assyrian, at 20 to 1, got home in ’B2, and the favourite burst
up as favourites almost always do. In ’B3 the public won
its money back on Martini-Henry, and in 1884 it piled the
winnings on Commotion, and when Malua was victorious
the result was general disaster. The miserable outsider,
Sheet-Anchor, took the pool next year, and about a dozen
horses that were dead certainties every one, never got a
show. Trident stood at 2tol in 1886, and Arsenal won.
Dunlop had been heavily backed on several occasions till
backers grew weary and disgusted, so in fBB7 he was
totally neglected, and with the inherent cussedness ®f the
horse tribe he triumphed on the only occasion when
nobody had any possible use for him. Mentor, the third
favourite, was the victor of ’BB, and then last year
Carbine and Melos divided all the money the nation could
spare, and the dead and forgotten Bravo extinguished
them both, and broke the bank for the twenty-sixth time.
* * #
And the moral of all this is that the horse is a
precarious animal, and backing is an insane superstition
unworthy of any white man’s consideration. Regarded
as a means of making money, it is more profitable to shoot
at a half-crown at 2000 yards’ range and lay even money
on hitting the coin in the centre, and as a measure of
economy it pays better to throw sovereigns through a
ladder on the understanding that the ones which stick to
the steps will be returned, while those that fall through
will be donated to the Imperial Institute. The public
bets largely on tips, and the man who gives the tip
generally knows nothing at all in the first place, and is
almost always a liar in the second. The owner himself is
almost as void of useful information as the rest of the
sporting community ; the jockey is just a trifle more
ignorant than the outsider—when he wins he always says
that lie knew it all along, and when he loses he can
always prove that some unforeseen circumstance spoiled
his chance; and, as for the horse, he probably knows
something, but he invariably keeps dark. And, conse-
quently, so far as the crowd is concerned, betting is—
with the single excerpt of hanging—the most unprofitable
speculation in the market. When the bookmaker goes
broke, as he does now and then, it is the wealthy horse-
owner who has cleaned him out; the mass of small specu-
lators merely empty their coin into a bottomless pit
who seldom gives more than 10 per cent of it back.
* * *
“There’s many a slip ’twixt the Cup and the
tip.”
# * #
“ Dunlop ” Donovan is the latest victim of the
Victorian land-boom. It would seem that he could have
been ruined in time on the turf, however, for he declares
that during his three years’ experience as a horse-owner
he lost as many thousand pounds.

The V. R.C. Derby of Saturday last made the
seventh won by Tom Hales, three of them-the last-
being in succession. The same jockey has ridden 10
V.R.C. St. Leger winners, the last five being successive.

Mr. Brodribb?

Alterations have been made on Fleming!on
Racecourse. The public on the fiat are pushed further
back, right behind the Steeplechase-running. The V.R.C.
is ready to die with vexation because its tenure of the
course won’t allow’ charging for general admission, as at
Caulfield. Wherefore the pauper public is to lie cribbed
and confined. Imagine the club getting 100,000 two bobs
for general admission to the Flat!

# # #
The Goddard-Dooley fight on Saturday night
in Melbourne is still another instance of the fallacy of
taking “ lines” from the performances of boxers. Peter
Jackson, against whom Dooley never had the ghost of a
show, was unable to hurt Goddard, whilst Dooley had all
the best of Goddard until his condition failed him. In
the third round he felled Goddard with a right on tlie
jaw, and Goddard only just managed to last the round
out. The minute’s spell pulled the giant together, and
Dooley falling w r eak in front of such terrible odds of
weight and brute strength (not to mention brutality), bis
seconds threw up the sponge at the end of the seventh
round. Poor Dooley is the most unfortunate man who
ever put on a glove, Just too small to hope to cope with
success against the giants, and just big enough to
exclude him from the middle-weights ; if he fights at all
lie has to tackle the big guns. Dooley, however, says he
can come down to middle-weight, and if he can do so
without weakening, he holds the middle-weight cham-
pionship of the world at his mercy. As to Goddard, a
fight to a finish between him and Slavin should be worth
an ear to see. It would be like a collision between two
locomotives at full tear. What a prospect of glaring eye-
balls and grinning teetli is involved (to change the meta-
phor) in a meeting between these two fine savages !
* * #
The pious and moral Melbourne Telegraph
complains bitterly about the Jackson-Goddard assault and
battery, the reason being that neither of the men seemed
any the worse. It sent a humble and merciful Christian
to make notes of the carnal encouter, and that person
expected to see the air darkened by hair and teeth and
pieces of human wreckage, and fancied that Jackson
would drive both his feet into Goddard’s chest, and that
Goddard would have his mouth full of Jackson’s scalp and
ears ; and as none of these things happened the Telegraph
felt hurt. It was surprised and disgusted to find that
neither of the combatants was carted away to the hos-
pital, and that the floor wasn’t strewn like the leaves on the
strand with eyes and vitals. It was ever thus with the
meek religious paper. The sort of thing it pines after is
a fight in a butcher’s shop between a cannibal and a bull-
dog, the defeated party to be eaten by the victor, and the
winner to be flayed alive and then ‘hung when the pro-
ceedings are over.
* * *
Goddard’s brother has beaten Slavin’s brother.
Would a meeting between the big guns take a similar
ending ? Goddard is just the stamp of man to compete
successfully against Slavin.
There is talk of Burge and young Sandy Ross
meeting, but it really seems a pity for the pair to smasli-
up good gloves on one another’s" granite skulls. About
the only thing that would make any impression on them
would be well-spiked Boz. knuckle-dusters. We charge
nothing for the suggestion.
# # *
Larry Foley has now an aldermanic binjie.
Nevertheless, it wus quite a treat to see him shape in front
of clever Jack Mollqy at the opening of the Australian
Athletic Club. Barring Jem Mace, the writer never saw
a man in Australia stand up to his work like the ex-
champion. Standing squarely to his man, not losing a
fraction of his height, with his left ready for a lead, and
the right playing loosely across the mark ready for stop
or delivery, Foley “shapes” in a style which the best of
our modern boxers might copy with advantage.

Bay, was an old resident of Bathurst and largely identified
with local sporting matters. lie had hut recently in-
herited a considerable fortune left by his father, and was
about leaving for Scotland to enter into possession.

* # #
The Senior Double Sculls race was won on
Saturday, at Double Bay Regatta, by the Fast Sydney
Rowing Club, represented by W. Colliding and J. A.
Smith. For years the Mercantiles “ held a mortgage” on
this race, when George Brett and George Seale, or Brett
and “ Sammy” Martin had only to go out and win against
any of the other clubs. The senior club, the Sydneys,
for years past have been weak in scullers, barring Gerald
Kennedy.
* * *
The lady cricketers have given up wearing
cricketing shoes, because they did not afford enough
support to the ankles, and now they wear high-leg lacc
hoots of white canvas and tan leather, with black stock-
ings, over full short skirts, with a sash tied at the side,
and oyer all , a loose sailor blouse, cut low at the neck.
From which we gather that the female professional
cricketer is a sort of high-class leg-show,‘and that you
could pick a good 22 from the combined ballet-forces of
Australia. Good hint for some one.

Dalton has swum from Blaokwall to Gravesend,
a distance of nearly 20 miles, with the tide (of filth) under
him on a still day in very slow time, but as he was very
much exhausted at the finish, it does not substantiate his
statement that he swam three times as far battling with
adverse tides and storms, in comparatively-speaking much
faster, time.

Story from Maoriland Going home the other
evening a friend furnished me with the following item.
Said lie : “ I was at a country meeting some time ago in
the North Island, and a, jockey was had up before the
stewards for suspicious riding, arid on being asked what
he had to say for himself, replied, pulling- out and ex-
hibiting a handful of totalisator tickets—‘ This is my
answer, gentlemen.-’ The answer, .too, was deemed satis-
factory, and young , well, young Johnny Arm-
strong, we will call him, might have been heard a few
minutes later telling his brother jockeys (who appeared
to chuckle at his smartness) that the stewards were a lot
of mugs, as all the tickets he held in his hand were on
another race;”

THE BULLETIN (Published at Sydney, N.S.W)
The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
The Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literary
Journal, is the only paper which has a con-
sistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Foi'm of Government,
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land,
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics'.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
ind follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for high-class printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form-printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-fire
national progress : it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
wealth, power, and privilege—the direct cause of
half the misery which now afflicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exercise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised, The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of the
ntelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more than a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed out-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The
Bulletin’s popularity is found in the unchallenge-
able fact —easily ascertainable from any postal
official—that, of all journals issued in Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mailed from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
