Tales of “The System,”
No. XVII.—“In Floggers’ Corner.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Floggers’ Curner at Sydney, Bathurst,
Newcastle and Moreton Bay. F logger’s Corner
at all other penal stations in His Britannic
Majesty’s prosperous and loyal Colony and De-
pendency of New South Wales in the year of
Grace, 183 . For, at the first mentioned places,
the Official Scourger was in the plural number;
elsewhere he was singular. Business was so brisk
at the Capital, the two leading country towns and
the Far North Settlement, that the Establishment
was under the necessity of putting two, and
sometimes three scourgers on its pay-sheets for
each of those centres of the reformatory activities.
At the smaller muster-stations and gaols, how-
ever, such as Wellington Valley, Goulburn,
Appm and Blackheath, one scourger’s services
sufficed to meet the ordinary demand for the
“cat. ’ Occasions of special pressure at the latter
points were met by “emergent engagements,” as
the official phrase ran.
The System worked most of its humbler hire-
lings altogether regardless of their requirements
of rest and recreation. It kept, for instance, a
convict constable on duty for 12 hours daily. But
those indispensable servants, the floggers, were
treated with a leniency approaching to loving
kindness. No scourger was asked to labour for
more than three or four hours on one day. His
muscles relaxed if exertion was demanded from
him for a longer period, and, though his temper
might grow fiercer and his will more fixed in the
faithful performance of his duties, an increasing
ferocity of disposition was worth nothing if his
arm grew weaker and his blows lighter. There-
fore it was understood (and specifically ordered)
that Messieurs the Scourgers should not be over-
taxed.
They could not do justice to their task, if they
were over-worked. The first recorded occurrence
of a strike in connection with any A'hstralasian
industry was the refusal of Dougherty, the illus-
trious Hobart Town hanger and scourger, who,
haying been actively engaged in whipping
prisoners from “sun-up” to noon one day,
objected to continue the pleasant duty into the
afternoon on the ground that “his strength
wasn’t equal to it.” And on the circumstance
that Mr. Dougherty was never thereafter requested
to exert himself after his mid-day lunch was,
according to scourgers’ tradition, founded the
System’s tenderness to their class. Dougherty had
induced the System to perceive that “it was as
bad as killing the goose that lays the golden
eggs” to work a fiogger to the extreme of
exhaustion, for what the dickens was the
good of sentencing a prisoner to be stripped
and whipped if the scourger was incapable
of inflicting punishment?” Which quoted sen-
tence the gentle and, humane reader will under-
stand is not the present writer’s, but is composed
of the ipsissima verba of a highly-esteemed Sys-
tematiser— E. A. Slyde, Esq., Police Magistrate
of Sydney, and inventor of the “improved cat.”
Slyde was a religious man and a churchman of St.
James’, but the Cross claimed but the smallest
share of his devotion. He preferred to swing the
censer of his adoration before the triangles. A
brilliant son of the System was Slyde; and it was
owing to his keen perception of things as they
should be that the regulation forbidding the over-
working of scourgers passed the Executive
Council.
*# * .
By virtue of that regulation the apostrophe
at Bathurst was shifted; and Fiogger’s became
Floggers’, and Mr. Cracknecke, Artist in the
Scarification of Prisoners’ Backs, received the
reinforcement of Mr. Chuckem’s services.
Both had been kept pretty busy for many weeks
prior to that August Monday when the Bathurst
Magistrates held their Field Day,* and their
muscles and limbs were, consequently, in fine
order. The former, in their admirable tension
were strung to concert-pitch, and the latter were
superbly flexible. The fiogger, who knew Ids
business, didn’t operate with only one part of Ids
body, and one particular set of muscles. In
rotation he brought several different parts into
play. Now it was the wrist, which inspired the
blow ; now it was the fore-arm; now it was the
tendons of the back that had to bear the strain.
By thus changing the seat of propulsive force, the
Artist in butchering was not only enabled to
economise his strength, but could inflict a distinct
variety of pain on the subject of his skill. Work-
ing from the fore-arm only, a scourge could do
nothing but lacerate the back and right side.
Utilising the wrist, he could introduce some
charming variations —we are speaking the lan-
guage of the System—of touch and going design.
And if he possessed a pliant waist, he commanded
the one remaining facility necessary to the reduc-
tion of the culprit’s exposed flesh to a mass of
palpitating pulp,
# * #
Cracknecke and Chuckem knew their busi-
ness. Cracknecke had taken to the vocation as a
duck to water. He was a born scourger. Chuckem,
on the other hand, though now an unquestionable
proficient, had to acquire excellence by degrees.
When he was appointed to Bathurst as Mr.
Cracknecke’s assistant, his touch was, in his
chief’s opinion, crude and inartistic.
“Yer not too proud ter take er ’int, beyer,
Chuckem ?” asked Mr. Cracknecke of his new
subordinate.
“Not a bit, sir, ’’ replied Chuckem, who viewed
Mr. Cracknecke with the becoming appreciation
of spirit which modest novices in an art should
regard its great masters. “Too ’appy, sir, if
yer’ll be so good,”
“Ri’te yer are, sonny, don’ be ’bove lamin’.
Now, w’en th’ nex’ cove’s up furmed’sun I’ll show
yer ’ow ter click outer his ribs. Yer rather weak
there, Chuckem.”
The next time a poor devil was “ up” for such
hygienic comfort as could be administered by the
cat-o’-nine-tails (his “dose” was “a Botany Bay
dozen” — Anglice, twenty-five lashes) Mr. Chuckem
Was the chastening agent of the day. but Mr.
Cracknecke generously abandoned some of his
well-earned leisure to the supervision of the new
hand’s efforts. , .
Mr. Cracknecke’s speech before the flogging
began was affluent with suggestions, and lie
stopped the punishment twice or thrice in order
to emphasize attention to a particular point-
“ Yer fitting too high, Chuckem.” “ Yer wastin’
yer tails, Chuckem—yer on’y fitting wi’ six or
seven.” “Yer fitting too low, sonny—yer a-
catchin’ him on his legs, an’ he don’ feel so
much, Chuckem. Besides, trousers is Gov’munt
pmp’ty, an’ yer mus’n’ destroy Gov’munt prop’ty,
Chuckem.” These were some of the gems that
coruscated in Mr. Cracknecke’s talk, and so
precise, so educative were they, that it is sur-

that a criminal was flayed a little more and a little

S °Mrf George Suchor, of Baulkham Hills, grazier,

Pugnacious Dr. Lang used to say that the offi-

It is a never-to-be-suffioiently regretted fact that
they did not hang together, and as high as
Haman.
# • *
We have purposely made the foregoing
digression from the main current of our narrative
in order that there shall not be the slightest
doubt as to Messrs. Crackneoke and Chuckem’s
competency for the task of distributing 1825 lashes
as per order of the Bathurst Bench.
Eighteen hundred and twenty-five lashes !
Nine times 1825 equals 10,425. Sixteen thousand
four hundred and twenty-five distinct outs to be
deposited on the pulsing bodies of between thirty
and forty of His Majesty’s subjects! — a heavy
job !—an onerous task ! But, thanks be to the
Evil Deities! —Messrs. Crackneoke and Chuckem
are equal to it.
* # #
The triangles stood in the comer of a
spacious yard. Facing i t was the entrance-gate
which opened on to the path leading to —and
from—the Court House. Three sides of the yard
were enclosed by a brick wall. The fourth was
bounded by the Superintendent’s house, two
windows of which overlooked that grassy Male-
bolge.
The hideous tripod was erected in the centre of
of an almost bare plot, twenty yards square, it
might be, in area. Its legs were young gums of a
foot’s girth, sunk three feet into the earth. Their
projecting ends were bound together with hoop-
iron. Lashings of the fibrous kurvajong or the
English hemp might do for smaller places, but
here, where the Sconrgef was a permanent institu-
tion, iron held the triangles together. Had hemp
been used, constant writhings would necessitate a
frequent replacement of the rope. A cross-bar of
six-inch batten held two of the legs together.
Floggers’ Corner was almost bare. The rest of
the yard, save the pathway, was from year’s-end
to year’s-end a grassy glory. You, dweller in the
Bathurst of the present, know that delightful
sward that is before your stately Law Courts—
how delightful it is to the eye and to the touch of
the foot. But it is nothing—as old visitors who
date from the thirties will tell you—to the yield-
ing verdure that carpeted the Punishment Yard
from the “ Super’s. ” wall to the line of holes on
the margin of Floggers’ Corner,
Floggers’ Corner was almost bare. Not com-
pletely so, for springing up between the sloping
Ifgs of the triangles were huge tufts of gristly
grass. They had escaped the scythe in the mowing
season ; the knife could not sweep between the
legs, and thus year by year clumps of thin, shiver-
ing, reddish stalks lingered in the blasting shelter
ot the skeleton structure. It is no fancy that
confers a blood-tint on their blades, but sober
fact. Whether it was that it imbibed from the
ghastly showers, which fed it constantly, some of
their ruddiness, or whether it was that subtle
quality which naturalists call mimicry, and which
leads many a plant to take upon itself the hue of
its surroundings, one cannot say; but certain it is
that the grass in the Corner gleamed with a dusky
blood-red, and not with the fresh verdancy of the
other portion of the Yard.
* * #
On this August morning the first blush of
an early spring had fallen upon the enclosure. A
petulant rain had ushered in the day and with it
had come the beautiful new year. An elm in the
angle next the super’s house that, yesterday, was
rude and sharp against the western sky, now sent
forth countless tiny shoots, and upon every dimi-
nutive point glistened a trembling diamond. From
the luminous wet of the grass walk peeped specks
of colour, the vanguard bannerets of the coming
host of wild flowers. In a box on the sill of the
superintendent’s window the minature volcano-
cups of the crocus were bursting into bright gold.
And over all, the delicious warm air hung with a
rapturous buoyancy that lifted up the soul as does
the sightless song ”of the lark. Even into the
noggers hearts penetrated some of the priceless
beauty of the new-born Spring.
Lor ! ” said Cracknecke, as he was tying up
the assigned man of Thomas Coxon, Esq,, J.P.
Am tit a stunner of a mornin’. Ido feel in
good trim to-day !”
* * *
A “stunner ” the day was, and he proved
,„ a “stunner,” too, before the final
I ally! was shouted by the constable. And
F 1 n °bly _ did Assistant Chuokem second the
brilliant achievements of his superior. Surgeon
Come had witnessed many scourgings before
that morning, and many a score afterwards. But
he marked that day with a white stone in his
calendar. The workmanship was of unsurpassable
excellence!
.Did Mr. Cracknecke tickle one man’s ribs to
perfection, Mr. Chuckem “ kissed ” great wheals
on the same part of the next culprit’s anatomy
with an equal facility. For Chuckem, you must
be informed, had bettered his master’s tuition in
one branch of the art. As a rib-tickler, the
Establishment had not another like him.
, They relieved each other as each “dozen” was
tallied by the Constable.
Did Mr. Chuckem score parallels on the shoul-
Ti,? rS T 1 ' ns first twenty-five out of a “ hundred,”
Mr. Cracknecke gashed the lower back with equal
symmetry. Did Mr. Chuckem cross, for the third
twenty-five, his former cuts with an approxima-
tion to accuracy, Mr. Cracknecke in the last
‘dozen,” would go one better. He would attain
accuracy, and traverse the marks he had previously
left so correctly that if you had measured one of
the minute squares which he had cut on the flesh
you would have measured nearly all.
Did the Senior cut up, the Junior cut down.
And the jets of blood gushed as profusely for the
latter as tor the former. Did the Superior change
the cut from his right-hand to left in the middle
of a current “dose,” the Subordinate would follow
suit. Leading off with his right, he would finish
his dozen with the native grace of a left-hander.
And there _ was practically nothing to choose
between their respective expertnesses.
It was altogether a fine performance! And not
the least admirable feature about it was the
absence of every mean element from their rivalry.
What Chuckem lost by an inartistic touch on the
shoulders or small of the back, he erained on the
ribs. He did not envy Mr. Cracknecke, and the
latter gentleman was of too magnanimous a
nature to view his assistant’s success with a judi-
cial eye.
When they adjourned for lunch, at 1 p.rn., they
had completed more than half of the day’s work,
and had polished off nearly all the morning batch
of offenders.
As they were passing out of the gate to the
Ordnance quarters, where they were to refresh
themselves, Dr. Corrie tossed them a shilling a-
pieced
“Here, lads,” he said, “get three fingers at
Mother Dillon’s. You’ve done splendidly this
morning, by George, but the magistrates, I hear,
will have some more for you this afternoon, so
keep your strength up. Hope you don’t feel
tired ?”
“Speakin’ fur mysel’, doctor,” Answered Mr.
Cracknecke, pulling his forelock in gratitude for
the tip, “I’m as fit as a fiddle.”
“Same ’ere, sir,” echoed Chuckem; “I ain’t
turned an ’air.”
The doctor nodded appreciatively, and, casting
a last glance around—perhaps to gain a whet for
his appetite—went likewise to his meal. He saw,
between the tripod, the spikes of grass glistening
red in the gay sunlight; from the spot where the
victims had stood five, six, seven narrow runlets
of ruby fluid going this way and that way ; frag-
ments of skin and morsels of flesh dotting the
Corner and decorating the lawn. His eye, prac-
tised in the niceties of surgery, even perceived a
minute section of a sinew lying, a quivering fila-
ment, on the beaten ground. He picked it up,
intending to use it for a microscopic object, and
placed it carefully in his pocket-book. And then
he went home for his midday refection and growled
at Mrs. Corrie because she gave him cold corned
beef. “I haven’t a proud stomach,” he com-
plained “but I do object to corned beef four
times a week.”*
# * *
Operations were resumed about 2.15 p.m.
The remanets of the morning charge-sheet were
cleaned off by two or three hundred dexterous
and sinistrous strokes, and the experts in torture
were granted a short spell. The doctor had got a
hint from inside the Court-house that the prin-
cipal afternoon “ case” was conducting himself
outrageously, and it was suggested to Corrie that
he should prepare the floggers accordingly:
“Boys, you’d better get the pickle-tub from the
Super’s yard, and wash your bats. There’ll be a
tough customer for you shortly. He’s a raw ’un,
too.” A “ raw ’un” was a prisoner whose acquain-
tance with the whip lay still in the future.
The men proceeded to obey the command) and
went round to the shed at the rear of the Superin-
tendent’s house, where the brine-tub was kept for
convenience sake. “ Mrs. Fry’s ’Liz,” the Super’s
nurse, was crossing the yard as they wete return-
ing. “Mrs. Fry’s Liz” had a history. She was
one of the women received at the Portsmouth
hulks from Cardigan Gaol with hoop-iron riveted
round the waist, wrist and ankles, and the great-
hearted lady had entertained fervent hopes of
Liz’s ultimate reformation. But Liz had been
sent to Mrs. Gordon’s lazar-house at Parramatta—
the System called the institution the Female
Factory and House of Correction—and after
her tutelage there the biggest fool of a Penal
Reformer who ever lived would not have given a
brass farthing for Liz’s reputation.
Liz hailed the scourgers, who were very old
friends of hers and attached to her by many ties
(Cracknecke had saved her once at the Factory
when he was Parramatta flogger, and she had led
a mutiny), with a gay “ Good day, mateys. Wot’s
the tub for?”
“ We’ve got a raw ’un to tickle,
An’ we’re a-goin’ th’ cat ter pickle,”
sang Mr. Cracknecke with a facetiousness that
would have done honour to the great Vance.
“Are yer, boys? Blest if I don’t bring th’
brats to look on. Th’ Super, an’ th’ Missus is at
Dimdale. The Snowlees’ givin’ a big dance to-
night.”
“Yes, do, Sis,” said Chuckem. “Th’ sooner
th’ kiddies Jams th’ System, the better.”
“Givin’a big dance, is they,” laughed Crack-
necke. “Wal, ol’ Snowlee’s made a lot o’ his ’ands
dance th’ ’ornpipe orlre’dy ter-day ! ”
By the time these “ brethren of the flagellant
persuasion ” (as John Macarthur once termed the
Official Scourgers, over the walnuts and the wine)
had got their tub and their cats properly soused
in the thick brine, the “raw’un” was waiting
their pleasure.
* * *
Dickson (as he called himself) or Dilby (as
Mr. Snowlee, J.P., had induced the Bench to
proclaim him) had been ordered 200 lashes.
Claiming to be a “ free ” man, Snowlee contended
he was an absconding convict, and in addition to
being sentenced to return to the iron-gang from
which, it was alleged, lie had escaped, the flogging
was inflicted for the triple offence of threatening a
magistrate, “refusing to answer truly certain
interrogatories as to his penal condition,” and for
committing a contempt of court. He had been
grossly violent in manner and speech in the
presence of the magistrates, and as he was hauled
out in the sunshine he still struggled and shrieked
furiously.
However, his strength appeared to leave him
when he was brought face to face with the
triangles. As the scourgers stripped him, the broad
hairiness of his chest heaved mightily with the
last throb of his spent passion, and then he fell,
limp and spineless, between the legs of the
machine.
The scourgers dragged him to his feet. He
threw himself down again crying, “God, have
mercy ! ” They pulled him up once more. “Be
a man, sonny,” said Mr. Cracknecke; “don’ be
beaten afore th’ cat touches o’ yer.”
They bent his hands together in their strong
grasp and passed their cord round his wrists pre-
paratory to binding them to the point of the
tripod. They pulled the string so tightly that it
cut the flesh. The pain revived his courage. He
drew himself together, filled his lungs with a great
in-take of breath, and, with an effort that thrust
knobs and gnarls of muscles on to the surface of
his back and limbs, snapped the cords. Dashing
his right fist into Cracknecke’s face he leapt
towards the gate. He Had covered the twenty
yards which lay between the entrance and Floggers 1
Corner when the soldier who stood sentinel
there, flung his musket across the aperture.
He overturned the soldier in a second, but
the momentary delay bad served the cause
of Law and Order very well indeed. It gave
another man of the military guard—one sergeant
and eight privates were on duty—time to recover
from his share of the astonishment into which
Dilby , alias Dickson’s bolt had thrown the group
of officials and spectators. He fired at the escapee,
and the bullet, at less than thirty yards’ distance,
could scarcely fail to miss its mark. It brought
the prisoner down.
Corrie went to the groaning, bleeding wretch to
examine him. “In the shoulder; it’s nothing
serious ; punishment can go on,” was his decision.
And it went on.
* * *
The blood flotved copiously fot soine
minutes from the bullet-wound and bedyed the
wretch’s naked flesh and trousers by the time
they had fixed him tip for his dose.
Craoknecke tore a sleeve ofit of the victim’s shirt:
the garment was not “ Gov’meht prop’ty,” so there
was no objection to his destroying it; and, dipping
the cotton in the pickle-tub, washed the gore away.
“ I’m goin’ ter do th’fancy trick, doctor,” he
said, viciously, “Air I Wan’a clean sU’face ter
Work on. ”
That drive in the face of Scourger Cracknecke
proved a most ill-advised proceeding of prisoner
Dilby. To the fire of Mr. Crackneeke’s artistic
delight in his work the blow had added the fuel of
a personal insult. Even a Scourger had moral
feelings that would be outraged by a blow. The
System admitted that much. What the Sys-
tem would never admit was that a prisoner had
any moral sensibilities,
Scourger Cracknecke worked his fancy trick to
admiration. He embellished the “ surface ” with
three strips of hanging cuticle, and by the deftest
of wrist-actions left one huge drop of thick gore
gleaming on the top of every pendant of skin.
Not more than half a dozen of the scores of
scourgers attached to the Penal Establishments
of N.S.W. and V.D.L. could compass that deed.
The mastery of a cool, calculating brain over a
pliant hand, and the exquisite delicacy of touch _
which dug each cracker of the nine-tails into the
human flesh, and in the same movement peeled
downwards nine strips of cuticle, were powers
rarely possessed. Cracknecke had them to per-
fection. With three successive fang-like strokes of
the whip he had punctured the back with the
triple row of holes, had withdrawn the single
clots, and left the hanging shreds of skin to
shrivel and curl.
“Lor !” exclaimed Chuckem, in an ecstacy of
admiration for his chief’s prowess, “I gives yer
best now, Mr. Cracknecke. I couldn’t come near
thet if I ’ed a show !”
The rows of hanging skin-strips had been
lifted several times by an upward stroke of the
lash ; a gust of the rather chilly breeze had also
rustled them once or twice; and the flesh on the
shoulders and ribs was rising in deep, corrugated
ridges before Dickson gave mouth to his agony.
But once his fortitude broke down, Heavens !
the heart-rendering poignancy of his cries, the
furnace-glare of his hollow eyes, the terrible con-
tortions of his body ! Again and again he threw
himself against the cross-bar ; again and again it
seemed as though his lower limbs, fastened as
they were with thin cords to the legs of the
triangles, would burst their bonds ; again and
again the tripod was on the very verge of
being uprooted from its socket; again and again
he cried for mercy, invoked God, Christ, the
Devil!
Let him writhe and shriek as he will there is no
mercy nigh unto him. At least, so it seems. For
Cracknecke finishes his first twenty-five, and
Chuckem his first twenty-five ; and Craoknecke
relieves Chuckem, and Chuckem Cracknecke;
and still the constable shouts his “ Five-ten—
fifteen— twenty — tally/” and still Dr. Corrie goes
up, as each “dozen ” is tallied and feels his pulse
and nods to the flogger ! Mercy ! Ah, no ! Not
if the System knows it!
And yet it would seem, too, that as there was
mercy for the thief on the cross, so there is mercy
for the victim knotted to the triangles.
For, at the one-hundred-and-twenty-first blow—
while the cat was still swirling with a dreadful
hiss through the air—while Cracknecke’s body was
still swinging round the hole in which the flogger
placed his heel to gain a greater purchase for his
stroke —while Dr. Corrie was just forming the
conclusion that the prisoner was “ going to see it
through ’’—-while Chuckem was making up his
mind to imitate the famous fancy trick on the
man’s ribs—while these things were severally
happening, Dickson’s head fell backwards, and
his silent but frothy lips showed he had fainted.
Cracknecke —we will do him justice^-tried to
check his blow, but he was throwing such an
enthusiastic force into his work that he could not
do so. The swirling cat fell on the upturned face.
“ Cast him off,” cried Corrie, The doctor was
as humane as the System made ’em; and he did
not believe in cutting a man’s face to bits. A
back, yes ! A face, no !
A grim silence fell on the loathsome group, as
many hands fumbled and bungled at the knots.
It was broken by merry, childish laughter, and
the delicious lisp of baby accents, perfectly audible
in the clear air :
“ Oh, p’itty, p’itty !”
All looked round. From the Super.’s windows
were gazing with rapt eagerness and a joyous light
in face and eye, the Super’s children, and the
youngest, a baby-boy, was dancing in the arms of
nurse Liz, and doing its best to carol an encore for
the pantomime which had charmed it, and its
brothers and its sisters, so greatly.
Well, what can you expect I If you put Cherubs
♦Possibly, (but I am not sure) Mr. Corrie’s fastidious-

# * *
Before Messrs. Cracknecke and Chuckem
fin illy pickled their cats that evening, they added
to the 225 lashes they had already given Mr.
Coxsan’s assigned man, his final 25. He took
them like a lamb. “Yer needn’t stay for me,
doc., he said to Dr. Corrie, “I ain’t a-goin’ ter
faint.” “ I have no intention, my man,” plea-
santly answered the surgeon, “ I’m not supposed
to supervise a flogging for less than fifty. This 25
forms no part of your last lot, you know—it is a
separate sentence and stands by itself.” Punc-
tilious Dr. Corrie!
The twin scourgers were clearing up before seek-
mg the sweet sanctity of their quarters. Chuckem,
with a besom, had brushed together a heap of
ghastly fragments.
“Yer can save yoursel’ th’ tru’ble, Chucky,”
said Cracknecke; “look at them ’ere ants.” A
countless host of these diminutive but exemplary
creatures were already banqueting on the viands
provided for them by the tender solicitude of the
System.
And the two worthies left the results of their
labours to the ants and a butcher’s dog, who
lapped in the runlets. As they wended their way
to the Ordnance Barracks they impressed very
palpable footprints on the sands of time and .on
the Bathurst pathways. For, no more than their
victims, could they keep their feet from being
soaked with blood. It oozed out of their boots as
they walked.
# * *
One last word. When, a month later,
Dilby (as the System contended he was— Dickson,
ns he said ho was) was presented the
most lamentable of objects ! —to the Overseer oi
Appin Road-gang as an absconder, that official
declined to receive him. He would have only
been too happy to oblige the Bathurst magis-
trates, he intimated, by accepting Dickson
as the escaped convict Dilby, but, really, he
couldn’t! He had himself recaptured the real
Simon Pure—the veritable Dilby— a week prior to
Dickson’s flogging. That was why.
PRICE WARUNG.
Humbug Rex.
[Fob The Bulletin..)
My lieges have built me a palace of marble,
With angels and cherubim carven in stone,
Symbols of honour and phrases that garble
'The, What-should-have-been, from the, That-
which-was-known:
“By each one that knew him beloved and
respected,
A model of virtue, whose loss we deplore ;
No promise forgot and no duty neglected,”
Are legends that cover its walls by the score.
Its halls are embellished with objects of vertu,
Floral devices are scattered around,
The subtlest craftsmen contribute a share to
The wonderful products that in it abound—
Book-dedications ; bought testimonials;
Addresses of welcome and marks of esteem ;
Trumpery titles for ambitious “ colonials,”
Autographs, photographs, puffs by the ream.
The homage of millions I’m daily receiving,
Their fealty showing in various ways,
Adventurers, sycophants, deceived and de-
ceiving,
Lickspittles’ grovelling, unmerited praise.
Selfish philanthropy seeking advertisement;
Disinterestedness scooping the pool;
Slimy hypocrisies, serpent-like blandishment,
Pound-saving piety, Satan’s best tool.
Parsons grow wealthy and censure rapacity ;
The legalised empiric rails at the quack ;
The famous star actor, with brazen audacity,
Bolsters his name with a wage-earning claque ;
The lawyer, appearing for Shylock the creditor
(In land-booming swindle he’s up to his neck),
Inveighs against fraud; and the unbiassed editor
Smiles as he pockets the syndicate’s cheque.
Charlatan demagogues,
A ladder to mount on and then kick aside,
Sit on the height while triumphant plutocracy
Rough shod o’er the millions supporting does
ride, .
The eight-hour top-sawyers for overtime struggle,
Then swell the gay pageant where bright
banners wave,
Deny themselves kin to (sophistical juggle)
Their poor sweated brothers, necessity’s slave.
Hark ! The great organ my anthem is swelling,
The cannon’s salute and the roll of the drum,
With clatter of trappings -and trumpet’s voice
telling .
’Neath flower-clad arches m triumph I come.
The pennons are streaming from tower and
st^Gpl©,
Heads are ’uncovered, the bells clash and ring,
Is heard in hosannas the voice of the people
Proclaiming great Humbug eternally King !
Bayville-st., Balmain.
M. A. G.
It has long been customary in England
not only to pay waiters no wages in establish-
ments where the customers are liberal, but actually
to sell for a premium the privilege of waiting.
The waiters have now formed a union and intend
in future to demand regular wages.

ablest man among the N.S.W. Opposition, that
he has given evidence of more astute ability, a
broader grasp of political questions, and a clearer
insight into the rules that ought to regulate public
life than any other Oppositionist.’' The old man
has sense enough to despise his adulators.
Miraculous Gladstone has just taken an-
other jaunt through Midlothian, recalling his dash
there in 1879, when he dished Disraeli. His
doctors then said it would snuff him out, more
especially as the time was approaching midwinter,
viz. Nov. 20th or so when he left Liverpool, and
Dec. 6th when he returned to Hawarden, but the
veteran was hardy as the Numidian Lion s nerve.
Ho went through a course exciting enough to
knock down a first-class man of thirty. Now he
charges upon Salisbury with the like vigour, and
who knows if he may not topple over another Tory
Palace of cards? Go'd old Samson, grappling the
Philistine pillars ; the debris shows a mixture of
crowns full-bottomed wigs, property robes of
purple, crimson, and those for the scarlet ladies,
all kinds of upholstery, gim-erackery, a tumble-
down rush of old dusty entails, primogenitures in
bushels, etc., etc, burying and stifling all the con-
stituents of Debrett,' Dods, Sir Bernard Burke,
Norroy, and the Garter King-at-Arms. The silver
tongue is placidly coffined, but its clarion has
doomed Jericho walls.

Several of our contemporaries persist in
speaking of “ the may on the Snakes of Ireland
which left off by saying : ‘ There are no snakes in
Ireland.'” Asa matter of fact, the reference is
to a hook on Iceland (we forget by whom; it is
about twenty years since we saw it) which con-
tained a chapter on Icelandic snakes, under which
heading the sentence: “There are no snakes in
Iceland ” stood alone in its glory.
John Ward, a working-man candidate for
Wandsworth, was asked : “ What will you do
when you get in the House? How will you live?”
“Well,” said Ward (a great, bluff artisan), “I
expect I shall have to pawn this coat to get there,
and turn up at St. Stephen's in my shirt sleeves.
I shall stand on the floor of the House, and as the
evening draws on I shall say, ‘ Mr, Speaker and
hon. members, what accommodation are you going
to provide for John Ward ? I have not got any-
where to go to, and I think of putting up here for
the night !’ ”

Says Hugh O. Pentecost
Now that the great strike is in everybody’s mind, it is a
good time to get one thought clearly defined. It seems
to me undeniable that an employer of labour should Con-
tend for the right to hire or discharge any employe
according to agreement made between the employer and
the as individuals. The idea that an outsider
should have the right to meddle in any way with such a
transaction is absurd. If this cannot now be done, as it
cannot, without injustice and suffering to the employe, it
is because the employed is at a disadvantage with the em-
ployer. That disadvantage arises from the fact that the
cannot get at land to support himself. It vacant
land were free the employe could at least support himself,
and if money and trade were free he could, without an
employer, live in luxury. What needs to be done, then,
is to make vacant land, money', and trade free. A labour
organisation is mere child’s play. It call do nothing
while the is at such a hopeless disadvantage with
the employer.
Editor Bulletin. Sir, —In the Queen r.
Harvey, the prisoner was convicted of inflicting
grievous bodily harm. The N.S. W. Full Comt
quashed the conviction on the ground that the
prisoner inflicted grievous bodily harm without
intent. In the Queen v. Bennett, decided on
Friday, the prisoner was convicted of the same
offence as Harvey. The Full Court upheld the
conviction on the ground that the prisoner in-
flicted grievous bodily harm without intent. Both
prisoners were convicted under the 24t!i section of
the Criminal Law Amendment Act. Is this in-
telligible or arguable? Peaceful but playful
citizens beware ! If the time of the Court was
taken up for two hours “ wantonly, wilfully and
unintelligibly,” why was not counsel peremptorily
stopped? Had not the Court power?—-Yours,
&c., A Puzzled Lawyer’s Clerk. Nov. 3, 181)0.
It appears that not even the magnificent
rent-roll of young Earl Dudley (who was in Aus-
tralia a little while ago) supplemented though it
be by the savings of a long minority, can keep
pace with the rate at which the young Earl is
living. The proceedings in the Dublin Nisi Prius
Court, when a Mr. Daly obtained a writ against
the Earl of Dudley, could take no one by surprise
who had followed the courses of the young man
since he attained his majority. “ Hardly a week
has passed without his spending thousands upon
horseflesh alone; and, as a rule, he has been
sigularly unlucky in his purchases. The writ is
issued in connection with Royal Meath, a horse
Lord Dudley bought in Paris for £7OOO from Mr.
Daley, He has already paid £2OOO, but finds
difficulty in raising the other £5OOO. Mr. Daly
refused to wait, hence the present action. The
Earl is, however, far too rich a man for there to
be any fear of things being allowed to go to ex-
tremities.”
Visiting Adelaide, the saintly appearance
of George Walpole Leake, Q.C., ex-P. M. and ex-
Judge, of Western Australia, has secured him the
offer of a captaincy in the Salvation Army. As
drummer “George” would more ably fill the bill,
for his aldermanic bread-basket would keep the
drum in perfect position. As Police Magistrate
and Judge in W.A. “George.” though always
feared, was never loved ; but as a wit, whether on
or off the Bench, he shone with a brilliancy
peculiarly his own. Once, however —it is the only
time on record—the old wag was Caught tripping
and completely floored. He was trying an ine-
briate named Thomas Green, and the name of the
culprit proved to him an overpowering temptation
to perpetrate a pun. “Thomas Green, ’ he said,
in lazy, careless tones, “you are charged with
being drunk, and,” he then added, “ I must say
you are very f/rccn to be here. ’ (Roars of
laughter.) Then the half-dazed, bleai-eved,
hoarse-voiced man in the dock leaned out of the
prisoners’-box and ejaculated ; “Yes, yer wuship,
Him green, and p’raps Him very green, but,
dash my buttons, Hi'm not has green has a leek.'-
“Dismissed, with a caution,” was the magistrate s
laconic reply. After that, George Walpole Leake
would as soon pick a pocket as make a pun in
court.
There is still living in W.A. an individual
bear in" the solrl'/iut of “ Moondync doe." who
was regarded, in the hoy-day of his convict-life,
as the champion prison-breaker of the world.
“ Joe ” has been immortalised in a novel written
by the late John Boyle O’Reilly, who. was his
fellow-prisoner ; and, truly, his numerous and
wonderful escapes from the “jug” have scarcely
a parallel in history. His would-be keepers even
put him in an iron cage for safe custody ; but the
iron cage was found empty one fine morning and
the gaol-bird flown. “ Moondyne,” like many of
his fellow-convicts, was the victim of cruel official
tyranny. Once he made a real clock out of card-
board —an exquisite piece of mechanism. The
warders saw it and hauled him before the prison
magistrate, who found him guilty of “ destroying
Government property,” and added six weeks to
his sentence. The “Government property re-
ferred to was three-quarters of a pound of old card-
board /
That poor little withered royalty, Albert
Victor, though he was born only a few
years ago, is already showing signs of extreme
old age, and fears are entertained that he will
break up from senile decay and leave a father and
a number of comparatively yuupg ancestors to
lament his loss. Then some time after he is gone
liis distracted parent will probably succumb to the
load of accumulated years and perish from second
childhood and other natural causes ; while the
Imperial grandmother of the family ploughs along
serenely in search of her next jubilee. It is one
of Victoria’s peculiarities that she is a great deaf
younger than most of her posterity. Wales, in
particular, looks as if he was his own grandfather
by mistake, and was weighed down by the innu-
merable responsibilities of his unnatural situation
as the remote predecessor of his mother and the
rest of the family.
The English people will, as The Bulletin
has long predicted, yet look with contempt and
disgust upon the very name of Brigand Stanley.
But then the J. Bull family are so dull-witted,
prosaic, egotistical, and greedy a lot that they
have simply been [marked out by Nature as the
perennial victims of hypocrisy. The mean, brutal,
pitiless face of Stanley alone ought to have been
enough, leaving his revolting egotism out of the
question, to proclaim him a fraud when lie began
to “ gag ” how he read the Bible in the wilderness
and what a mercy and a comfort it was to him.
Yet, as the British public unquestioningly accepts
as the accredited representatives- of the shoeless
and hungry Christ whom it pretends to adore, a
lot of paunchy impostors with thick underlips and
smug faces that are mere coloured maps of glut-
tony and greed and self-indulgence; a,s it has for
hundreds of years paid toll and tribute to a horde
of fat and insatiable vampires who travel in the
name of self-denial and charity—it is no wonder
that they, who worship at the feet of an unspeak-
ably dull and ignoble set of bogus gospel-mongers,
should fall easy victims to the wiles of a calculat-
ing Welsh-Yankee news paper-man-book-agent
who has sized ’em all up to a nicety. Ihe state-
ments made by Troup and others as to the ivory
business are but the beginning of the arch-hum-
bug’s downfall.

To the Head of the Long Film.
The long-expected come at last !
The Coalition’s day is -past,.
And, though the Gillies-Deakin firm
Has flourished for a lengthy term,
By selling friends and buying foes
On dfookerl bills, as Heaven knows, ■
E’en on the eve of Melbourne Cup
The order came to wind ’em up.
Adieu to Duncan’s nasty smile
For, please the pigs ! a little while ;
■ That grin of triumph, quite unique,
Which puckered-up his brazen cheek
And slowly softened to a sneer
Will, for the present, disappear,
And all his gas will be dispers’d
As from a toy-balloon that’s burst.
O, do you ever know remorse
For what you’ve done ? You don't , of course.
Could conscience e’er of slumber rob ,
The man who worked the Tramway job,
And fiftv more of lesser weight,
Such as the “ extra thou.” for Speight ?
No shadows of his shifty schemes
Will darken tough old Duncan’s dreams.
The long-expected come at last!
Your feast is followed by a fast;
Seek now to let the country know
It cannot trust “ that false Munro,”
Who’ll do the things you did yourself
Till Jimmy laid you on the shelf.
Rail loud against that man of sin
Except when you are “standing in.”
Be patient, Duncan ; brave, remain,
Your turn will soon come round again ;
In time you’ll get a decent show
To snatch the bag from J. Munro,
And purchase with your golden shower
Another spell of pomp and power.
Meanwhile, thank Fortune that your rest
is taken in a feathered nest.
Duncan Gillies has just finished his twelfth
year of office, the recently-collapsed Ministry being
the seventh with which he was connected. In his
early life he was an incidental disease, and every
Cabinet had him at least once, but now he seems
to have become chronic and incurable. As there
is no known remedy for him the country can only
take refuge in prayer. The only Victorian
Minister who enjoyed an equal record was J . M.
Grant, who was also progressing favourably in his
seventh Cabinet when he died. Like Gillies,
nothing short of death would ever have shifted
him R S. Anderson was in seven Governments
and "held office for 10 years altogether; he would
probably have been in 14 Governments only he
got buried somehow. Sir James M Cullooh had a
hand in six Cabinets lasting for an aggregate of
11 years, and then he collapsed like a dynamited
egg-shell, and from being the supreme boss of a
colony he fell into such absolute nothingness that
an up country municipality would have scorned to
appoint him as pound-keeper and local dog-
drowner. Berry and Kerferd belonged to six
Cabinets, and were eight years in office, and
Francis drew his salary as a statesman for nine
years and lasted through five Administrations.
An Administration was an ephemeral thing in
these days, however, and when the Opposition
called on it to “come outside” it generally went
out and failed to get back again.

Honi Soit Qui Mai Y Pense:
The Tale of a Garter.
Mr. Bertram Lamar sat on the arm of a chair in
the hall of the hotel and idly swung his foot. Mr.
Lamar was bored. An extended acquaintance
with this young man forces me to confess that he
was not often afflicted in that way. We know
that no man can aspire to be of the highest fashion
without constantly experiencing the pangs of this
distinguished, ailment, and yet Mr. Lamar was
unquestionably of the highest fashion. The most
cursory glance would tell you this. Anyone could
see that he belonged to the best people, that only
the best people would be bearable to him. It was,
therefore, with humiliation that he confessed to
himself how seldom, considering all his advan-
tages, he felt the inspiring throes of boredom.
Who can guess how hard he tried to crush his
exuberant vivacity, to quell his laughter, to extin-
guish the sparkle in his handsome brown eyes ?
He could find diversion at Tadmor in the Wilder-
ness. If you were a true devotee of fashion, his
unquenchable spirits would make your hair stand
on end.
. But now he was genuinely bored, and he felt
joyously that anyone could see he was. Ladies
passed—old ones, who swept up the dust with
trains, and who creaked as they moved, as if they
wanted oiling; young ones, in light frocks and
wide, flowered hats, which cast a shadow over the
clearest eyes. They kept banging open the glass
doors and going out into the blaze of sun beyond,
with a bursting into bloom of lace parasols,
or coming into the cool of the hall, with
the rustling, silken sheathing of the para-
sols suddenly furled, and the tapping of
their little heels on the hard floor. He never
glanced at them. But they did at him—swiftly,
obhquely-from under the hat-brim out of the
shadow. He looked away, with raised chin and
indolently drooped eyelids ; but about his heart
he began to feel a little creeping, warm titillation
of interest, a premonition that ho was not going
to keep bored very much longer. There was one
B-M-she kept going to and fro—and, as lie looked
on the ground, he could see the hem of her dress
and her feet. They were pretty feet in yellow
shoes, small and pointed. Mr. Lamar found him-
self ruminating : “ Suppose the head is as pretty
as the feet. But it never is. There’s a law of
compensation which prevents that. The head
which belongs to those feet is thirty-five” And
he looked up. The head matched the feet to perfoc-
tion. Mr. Lamar felt ta.it he did not look bored any
longer. Rather, however, than sacrifice this
dearly bought and enviable condition of being, he
turned his back on that enchanting head, and
sauntered into an adjoining room. There would be
no one to look at here to destroy the aristocratic
vacuity of his mind.
The room was empty, cool, and dim. It had
oak-chairs and tables and writing-desks, sea-green
walls, and a great window opening on the balcony.
Outside there Were old ladies sitting under a
forest of parasols. Beyond were velvety sweeps
of close-cropped turf, dappled with short shadows
shrinking to the tree-roots. Splinters of dusty
sunlight crept down the boles of the stately
cedars and trembled on the white dresses of
passing girls. There were glimpses of spiked
unfolding fan-palms and rustling melancholy
dracaenas, fat cactuses of an unhealthy morbid
hue, and little squat, flat, prickly green things
growing in irritable confusion. Mr. Lamar felt
that he might gaze upon this prospect for an
infinitude of time and remain bored.
But fate willed otherwise. As he entered the
room he saw something on the floor near the table.
He picked it up. It was a band about an inch
and a-half wide, covered with a puckered yellow
ribbon, and with one end run through a clasp of
dull silver, showing a monogram in small dia-
monds. There was a bunch of narrower yellow
ribbon beside the clasp, each end finished with a
little tongueless silver bell. It appeared to Lamar,
from some hanging filaments of thread, that the
two ends of this band had once been stitched
together. He looked curiously at his find.
“ What can it be ?” he mused, staring at it. We
have said that he was young, and came of the best
people, and with the best people there is always a
doubt as to whether they Wear such vulgar things
as stockings or possess such unmentionable things
as legs. “ Wings, not legs or feet, shall move
them,” as the pOet gracefully expresses it.
Lamar first; thought he would take it to the
office. But curiosity impelled him to study it.
It might be worn round the neck; but no; it
was not long enough. He drew the severed ends
together, and held it off from him, eyeing it
dubiously, and reflectively pulling his small
mustache. Oh, yes, of course. Now he saw.
How dense he had been ! A bracelet. Holding
it together, he pushed his hand through it, and it
swung on his wrist.
“ I don’t think I ever saw a bracelet just like
that_ before,” he thought, moving it round and
looking at it with his head on one side.
And then, as he looked at it, came a sudden
flash of wakening light, and, for a moment, he
stood staring at it in stupefied horror as it hung
over his wrist. With the return of consciousness,
he crumpled it up and crushed it into his pocket.
What should he do with it ? If he took it to the
office, its owner would never dare to claim it. If
he found out who she was, he would never dare to
offer it.
He could imagine the scene: A lovely and
youthful lady is discovered walking in the cor-
ridor. To her appears Mr, Bertram Lamar in
full evening-dress, with a white pink in his
button-hole. Making towards her, he executes
one of those majestic yet easy bows for which the
Lamar family have been famous ever since the
days of their illustrious founder. Then, drawing
a package from his pocket, Mr. Lamar presents
it to her, murmuring, “Yours, I believe,” and
vanishes through a trap-door. He might give
it to her, and then pretend to be prostrated by
the heat, and fall on the floor with his eyes shut,
to avoid embarrassing her by witnessing her in-
evitable confusion. The Lamars were also famous
for their chivalrous attitude towards the sex.
But can a man be prostrated by heat on a day
when the thermometer in its most ambitiously
soaring moments can only struggle up to seventy-
five degrees ? What should he do to spare her
feelings and his own ? And he turned the catise
of his perturbation over in his pocket.
Just then he heard a step outside —a feminine
frou-frou. With a guilty start, he retreated from
the table, fell into a chair, and seized the morning
paper, in which he buried his head. Any one
noting this fact would, of course, imagine that he
slumbered, and feel themselves safe from espial.
“ It is she,” thought Bertram, seized with guilty
tremors; “she has come to hunt for it,” and he
remained motionless.
So did she. There was not the smallest vibra-
ting rustle from her silent figure. Bertram rattled
the paper, stabbed a little hole through it with his
finger, and peeped at her. She was standing in
the doorway, peering about the room, and she
was the young lady with the yellow shoes. She
was charmingly pretty, in a light dress of striped
flannel and a loose shirt of thin silk, made like a
boy’s. Under the turned-down collar was knotted
a four-in-hand necktie of white pique, and about
her waist was a woven silk belt clasped with a
silver S. She was slowly sweeping the room with
a long glance, only her head moving, her figure
finely erect, her right thumb in her belt, and her
left hand hanging by one side and lightly clasping
a little leathern thong which was wound about
her knuckles. As to her head —that lovely head
with strong brown hair curling up crisply under
her sailor hat, delicately rounded cheeks, and
gravely pouting lips—it was an image of soft,
delicious beauty. At her side sat a little pug-dog
on its haunches, gasping and rolling its eyes.
She cast a hurried glance at the gentleman read-
ing the paper, and walked into the room, looking
intently about the floor.
“What would she say,” thought Bertram, as
she passed him in her search, “if I were to inno-
cently ask her what she’s looking for, and gallantly
offer to help her find it ? But I’ll spare her that,
even though I do appear a clown,” and the hero
peeped again.
She was certainly hunting thoroughly. She
moved several of the chairs, drew up the lace
curtains and looked under them, peered into all
the corners and under the tables and round the
desks.
“What the deuce could she have been doing ?”
thought the young man; “ she must have been
playing puss-in-the-corner or forfeits. I’ll burst
with curiosity soon and hit her with one of the
pieces ”
When she had searched everywhere she straight-
ened herself with a sigh, threw one last reluctant
look about the room, and calling to the pup,
“.Come along, dearest, it isn’t here,” departed.
Bertram laid down the paper and looked after
her. She appeared to him to have a singular
amount of sang-froid, also a very graceful back.
How well she walked ! How proudly she carried
her small dark head !
When she was out of sight, he cautiously drew
forth the yellow band and studied the monogram.
It was exceedingly complicated, and seemed to
him to consist mainly of large B’s, with small O’a
distributed about them, which puzzled Mr. Lamar.
Mr. Lamar was not bored that afternoon. He
was consumed with perplexity. How could he
return the lost treasure to its owner without caus-
ing her embarrassment, without making her his
enemy for life ? If it had been anything else, how
delightfully he could have broken the ice with it!
But to break the ice with that histrionic problem—
impossible ! It would have exactly the opposite
effect, and cause the ice to form as beneath a
breath from the Arctic.
He roamed about listlessly. It was a warn
afternoon, and outside the glass doors there was a
great blaze and glare of sun. After lunch, th®
guests dispersed. The dowagers lumbered upstairs
to appear resplendent at dinner-time, and sit
along the ball-room walls, watching three gyrating
couples try to imagine, like the ambitious little
goats who endeavoured to persuade themselves
that they were cows, that they were attending a
large and select ball of the most brilliant gaiety
and fashion. The younger married women also
withdrew, to lounge away the afternoon in golden-
moted gloom behind drawn blinds. Driving-
parties started out with laughter and gay voices,
the snapping of whips, and the grinding of gravel
under revolving wheels. Away they whirled down
the drive, streaked with already lengthening
shadows, to range along winding yellow roads
beside the many-sounding sea, where dark, writhing
cypresses crouched before the angry breezes, or
steal through noiseless, dim, dun-coloured woods,
silent and dripping with funereal mosses, the sad
ghosts of once green and whispering forests. Some
of the young ladies strolled over the lawn, away
through light and shade, parasol on shoulder,
book in hand.
Then it grew very hot and still. There was the
long roll and crash of balls from the bowling-
alley, and a gentle, occasional clicking from the
billiard-room. The clerk read a novel with his
feet on the desk; down in the barber’s shop the
barbers were all sitting in white jackets, reading
papers. In the glass-covered end of the balcony
enormous flies buzzed ceaselessly, and bees swung

in the cups of the huge drowsy-headed poppies.
The shadows stole across the velvet sward, a leaf
circled languidly downward through sun and
shade. Here was a condition of things to have
bored a newly-liberated convict, to have subdued
the high spirits of a miss fresh from the convent.
But Mr. Lamar was not bored. He even did
not care whether he looked so or not. His pride
was shattered. He sat on the balcony, and there
being no one to dispute his loneliness, he put both
his legs over the arm of his chair, clasped his
hands behind his head, and tilted his hat on to
the bridge of his nose. From under the hat-brim
rose occasional thin spirals of smoke and long
sighs.
“I must give it to her this evening,” he
thought; “ I’ll wrap it up in paper and tie one of
the ribbons round it that are on that mouchoir-
case Milly gave me. Then, if she asks me —as,
of course, she will —what it is, I’ll say, carelessly ;
‘Oh, nothing! .Just a little trifle, I think,
belongs to you. Don’t hurry to open it. Have
you noticed what a beautiful night it is ?’ And so
I’ll engage her in absorbing conversation. But if
the conversation is not sufficiently absorbing, and
she begins to open it, I must flee from the wrath
to come. And when next I meet her, dying to
speak or even bow to her, there will be a wall of
ice raised between us. She will turn her profile
toward me and become engrossed in the beauties
of the landscape.” Such is the irony of fate.”
The languorous afternoon burned itself away in
loitering stages of deepening light and colour to a
smouldering crimson sunset. Dinner loomed in the
near distance, and was awaited with fond ex-
pectancy. At 7 o’clock Mr. Lamar came slowly
down the broad stairs, looking as handsome as the
young Dionysius in his dress-suit, his shining
shirt-bosom, and a white pink in his buttonholO.
The hall was full of moving figures and a blaze of
light and colour. Gay running laughter and
broken ends of sentences detached themselves from
the hum of voices. Splendidly-dressed ladies
passed and repassed in couples, in threes, in long
lines, or stood in groups, with the gas-light break-
ing in myriad little splinters on their tumbled
trains of soft silk, and sliding with glossy
radiance along their half-bared arms. There was
a fluttering and sweeping of fans, a rustling of rich
crushed fabrics.
Outside there was still light. Through breaks
in the foliage gleamed the fiery eye of the angry
sunset. The summits of the taller trees were
touched with a lingering brightness, but in their
odorous shadows lurked the reluctant dewy dusk.
Mr. Lamar was too perturbed in mind to care to
mingle with the gay, loud-voiced, laughing crowd.
He wished for solitude, and directed his steps
towards the little writing-room. He had not
wrapped the treasure in paper, nor tied it up with
a ribbon from his mouchoir-ease. He had not
done anything with it. He did not dare. The
sight of its owner might inspire him to the des-
perate pitch of boldly offering it to her, or suggest
to him some cunning way of returning it without
betraying the identity of the finder. With these
ideas in his mind he carried it still in his pocket,
in company with his keys, jingling on the end of
a chain.
The gas in the writing-room was not lit. Red
gleams shot in from between the great boles of the
cedar-trees and touched the sea-green \valls. Mr.
Lamar liked the softening light and the pensively-
faint prospect, and went to the open window'.
Just outside it, on the balcony, in a Shaker rock
ing-chair, was the young lady who had worn the
yellow shoes. She was reading and rocking, her
pug in her lap; and if she was pretty in her
flannel morning-dress, words cannot describe
her in a mist of fine black gauze, cut
square round her neck, and showing her
arms to the elbow. Her skin was as white and
flawless as a blanched almond. There was the
gleam of a gold pin from the shadow of her dark
hair, and a jewel hung round her neck rose and
fell with her quiet breath. As she read, she
absently pulled the pug’s ears, which drowsed
with its eyes half-open and its head against her
arm.
Lamar looked. She turned the page. The pug,
disturbed, rose to its fore-paws, gazed at her with
an expression of idiotic fondness, and tried to lick
her chin. She avoided this demonstration of
affection by moving her chin from side to side,
keeping her eyes still on the book. The pug con-
tinuing, she struck it gently, observing :
“Don’t, you bad little abominable dog !”
“I beg your pardon,” said Lamar suddenly
from the window.
The lady looked up, with the raised eyebrows of
polite inquiry.
“I have something of yours,” said the young
man, desperately, and in a low tone.
“Yes? What is that?”
“I —I—don’t quite know. Or, rather —well —
but —um ! I didn’t like to leave it at the office. I
thought—yes—l thought ” He leaned out of
the window, with his closed hand extended.
“ Here it is.”
She held out her hand, and he dropped it in.
She looked, and gave an exclamation of joy that
caused the pug to jump to the ground.
“Oh, how glad I am! Thanks so much.
Thanks awfully ! I was so afraid it was lost.
Isn’t that lucky ? ” And she looked affectionately
at the returned treasure, with her head on one
side.
There was light enough to see her face dis-
tinctly. She did not exhibit a sign of embarrass-
ment, not the ghost of a blush. Lamar felt a
sudden chill of disappointment and disapproba-
tion.
“You found it in there?” she said, indicating
the writing-room, and looking up at him wi t
frank, candid eyes; “yes; that’s where it w a
lost.”
“I —I supposed so,” said Lamar, with a wan
smile.
“ I looked for it myself this morning all over,”
she continued; “ under everything; but it was
gone. ”
“Yes?” said the young man, with a fatuously
inquiring air. (“If she knew I was behind the
paper, she’d ask me why I didn’t give it to her
then and there; and what the deuce would I say ?
I couldn’t tell her that I was afraid she would be
embarrassed, for such an idea has apparently
never entered her head. I must dissemble,”
thought he.)
“ I value this very much,” she went on, turning
it over in her hand.
“ I should imagine so,”
“You see there is only one like it. There is
not a single duplicate anywhere.”
She looked smilingly in'to his face. Lamar
stared at her in stupefied horror.
“ Only one—did you say ?” he managed to
articulate in a faint voice.
“Only one,” she repeated, nodding her head ;
“it was made to order.”
There was a moment of silence. Lamar made
no comment, but continued to stare vacantly at
her. He was thinking, “It must have been an
accident. She can’t be a veteran of some war or
other. I might have known it—the law of com-
pensation again. Such a lovely face as that must
have some counter-balancing defect.”
“When you have only one—and that such a
pet,” she continued, not noticing his silence,
“you like to have everything as pretty as pos-
sible.”
“Yes, yes. Of course, of course,” ejaculated
Lamar, laughing idiotically. “If you have only
one, I expect it must be somewhat of a treasure,”
he thought. Then he added boldly, but with
the air of confiding a piece of news: “ I have
two.”
“Two?” said the young lady, with vivacious
interest; “what kind?”
Lamar looked askance at her, in alarmed silence.
Was she doubly afflicted ? She was stroking the
pug with the tips of her fingers, and there was
nothing in her placid expression to suggest mania
of any form.
“ The same as everybody else’s,” he answered,
with some hauteur; “ are the people in this part
of the country in the habit of managing with one.”
“As a rule, they have only one; it’s so much
less bother. Though, to be sure, I have a friend
who has—let me see—yes, eleven.”
“ She must be a centipede,” thought Lamar ;
“ I seem to be encountering remarkable freaks of
nature. There is a fortune waiting here for any
one who wants to start up a cheap museum.” Then
he remarked aloud, regarding her with his head
on one side, a tolerant, fond smile on his lips :
“ That must be quite an emharras de richesse,
especially when you’re walking.”
“They do get rather in the way,” admitted the
young lady; “ but most of them are very well
trained,”
“ Very clever of them, I am sure,” murmured
Lamar, leaning against the window-frame, and
feeling that he was about to swoon.
There was another short silence, during which
the girl continued to examine her restored treasure.
Presently she said, musingly : “I see the threads
are broken. She has broken them once before,
though I don’t see how she can possibly do it.”
Lamar only stared and swallowed. She held his
glance with a horrible, eerie fascination.
“You know she loves to run about so,” she
prattled on ? “ she ran away from me this morn-
ing, and when she came back it was gone. She
must have crept under the table, and not come out
until she had got it off.”
“ Who is she ?” asked Lamar, in a voice from the
tombs.
“She? Why, Bobo —my pug. Isn’t she a
beauty ? Come up here, Bobo ” —patting her
knee—“l want to put your collar on and show
this gentleman, who was kind enough to return
it, how pretty you look when you’re all dressed
up.” She held the band round the dog’s neck,
and, turning to Lamar, said, with laughing arch-
ness, “Isn’t it becoming?”
Lamar sat down on the window-sill. He was
not bored, but he took up the morning paper
which lay on an adjacent desk and began to fan
himself with it, though the evening had grown
cool.— Geraldine Bonner, in Habpeb.
The Psalm of Labour.
[For The Bulletin.]
Shall we toil in life-long sorrow,
Plod like bullocks in the plough,
Wear the galling yoke to-morrow
That our necks are wearing now ?
Shall the heel tyrannic grind us
In the dust as oft of yore ;
Each successive morrow find us
E’en more slavish than before?
Shall the white-hands, bleached our blood in.
Reap the harvest of our toil;
Ope the gate to let War’s flood in
On our virgin peaceful soil ?
Whose are these that rise to slay us—
Sabres drawn and Gatling guns ?
Knaves elected to betray us,
Traitors all, and Judas’ sons.
Lo, the bloody rites of Mammon
Nought but human lives appease:
Build the altar, lay the lamb on,
Rob the hive and slay the bees !
O, ye prophets of the dayspring,
And the promise from on high !
What can rulers such as they bring
To the weary souls that sigh ?
To God’s earth a famished larder ?
No ; His table's spread for all;
Though those sons whose lives are harder
Gather only crumbs that fall.
Austral sons of British sires—
Cast in freer moulds than they—
Rise and catch Promethean fire,
More of Christ and less of clay.
Hark ! a million voices call you,
Burst the bonds that hold you down,
Break the fetters that enthral you,
Grasp your sceptre and your crown !
Not through bloody revolution
Lies the royal road to Right:
But through patient evolution,
Saying “Let there first be Light.”
Show the worth of bone and sinew
And a strong arterial flow —
Fan the burning thought within you,
Dare to act, and dare to know.
Down with old-world race-dissensions,
Truth and justice lead the van ;
Creed and Caste are hell’s inventions s
Trust the brotherhood of man.
Down with cut-throat competition,
And the sturdy beggar’s call;
Down inordinate ambition —
All for each, and each for all.
Cling to truth though friends desert you,
And yon band lik.e Gideon’s grows
Weaker on the side of virtue,
Till you woo and win your foes.
Trust yourselves in hope of others,
Weaker vessels though they be ;
In the Ballot’s battle, brothers,
Stand united and be free.

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which shou d be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
v- nr. *
Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors.
Strictly Original mutter contributed to The Bulletin
will be paid fur. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under :
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
other a ' " Drawings must be made in clean lines
of imi t on white paper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished rcdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
h- Serious poems, of similar or 'treater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial Illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the 'paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

Is what the voice of Labour said.
The good old times have gone away,
The voice of Labour is here to-day.
Eight hours’ work and eight hours’ ease
And seek repose just when you please,
Such seems to be the latest rule
That is in vogue in Labour school.

And have your bottle of St. Louis beer.
