Australian Actors’ Grievances: “An Old
Hand” Discourses Thereon.
Dear Bulletin, —The question asked by yoa in
a recent issue, “Why do not actors strike?” is
one that merits more serious consideration than ifc
has hitherto received. As The Bulletin is
always on the side of justice as opposed to mere
power, a few facts as to the actual treatment of
the profession in Australia—facts, as a rule, totally
unknown to the outside public—may not be amiss.
It may be premised that although there would
be probably nothing more difficult to organise
than an actors’ union (without which any strike is
impossible), yet, that once formed, no union would
probably be able to exercise so much power. All
the conditions of complete success lie dormant in
the idea. But who will bell the cat ?
Actors, which term must be held to include
both sexes, and to apply for the purpose of this
letter only to those fulfilling a salaried engage-
ment under agreement, excluding the pseudo-stars
who, by virtue of the possession of plays of more
or less doubtful merit, wander to and fro to the
infinite detriment of dramatic art, may be divided
into two classes: those who are playing under
agreements signed abroad and those who are
playing under “ colonially-signed ” agreements.
There is very little difference between the two,
with the exception that the latter are signed more
or less under the compulsion of necessity with a
bitterly-acquired experience of all that they in-
volve ; while the former are mostly signed in
entire ignorance of the real circumstances of the
case. But, wherever signed, the tenor of the agree-
ment is the same, and they are purely one-sided
affairs ; the manager can violate the spirit or even
the letter with impunity, while the artist can as a
rule only suffer in silence.
Manager A goes to London for the purpose of
engaging fresh talent, or, perhaps, requests his
agent to do it for him. To an artiste, wearied
and, perhaps, ill with continual work, the
prospect of a long sea-voyage, with an engagement
at the end of it, is an attractive bait. The agree-
ment is always for twelve months, with the option
of renewal for another twelve months on the part
of the management only. Manager A, or his agent,
is all suavity, the salary offered is higher, too,
than the artiste is at present receiving— not very
much higher (in the ratio of £7 to £5 usually), but
still higher—and everyone knows that Australia is
a cheap place to live in. A printed form of agree-
ment is produced which provides inter alia that for
four weeks in the year should the artiste not be
performing no salary shall be paid. The actor, .
perhaps, objects that the agreement is ha dly
explicit enough, that it is entirely in favour of the
manager, and that, for instance, no provision is
made lor playing in his or her special line of busi-
ness. Manager A, or his agent, still all suavity,
points out that for various unexplained reasons
they are obliged to adhere to their printed forms,
that no difficulty can possibly occur, and that
every consideration will be shown to the wishes of
so well-known an artiste, &c., &c., and finally the
agreement is signed.
Arrived in Australia, the main points which the
actor discovers are, that from Manager A. all
suavity has disappeared, and that he is now pos-
sessed of just about as much oonside ation for
his employe as the boss director of a dock com-
pany displays to a lumper, that although the
salary paid is slightly higher than that paid in
England (in the provinces), yet, that the actual cost
of living is more than twice as much, that there
are no long runs, and in consequence continual re-
hearsals, and, more than that, there are continual
heavy expenses for dressing continual new parts
(the Australian public being very exacting in this
respect); further, the actors find themselves cast for
business for which they were never engaged, put
into pantomime or burlesque at the whim of the
management, or “lent” to support music-hall
stars or Irish comedians; further still, the month
without salary which the management stipulate
for is nearly always taken at the very commence-
ment of the engagement, so that unless an actor
starts with funds m hand, which is often not the
case, he must commence by getting into debt in a
strange country. Weighted with all these disad-
vantages the end of the year often sees the artiste
without sufficient funds to return home, and then
there is only one result, a result calculated on and
often deliberately brought about—he takes up a
permanent residence at Poverty Point, and be-
comes the slave of a “ring ” of colonial managers.
For “ring” it is in the fullest sense of the
word. Our actors are miserably paid and more
than overworked, and yet have no remedy,
for to quarrel with one manager would
generally be to quarrel with practically all and
deprive themselves of the means of earning a
livelihood. It is a well-known fact that one Aus-
tralian manager, should an artist for any just
reason refuse to fulfil his engagement, has the
incredible meanness to indite at once a letter to
his brother managers asking them never to employ
the said artiste again, a request that, on the
part of several of the fraternity, is more often
than not complied with. They have always taken
care that the supply shall exceed the demand, and
a sufficient number of artistes are always kept
walking about to supply the place of any recalci-
trant member of a company.
The question of overwork admits of no dispute.
Owing to the short runs usually obtained by pieces
out here the hours may be put down as follows :
Rehearsal, 10 till 4; dressing and acting, 7 till
11.30; in addition to this, somewhat more than ten
hours of the day, a considerable amount of time
must be given to the study of continual fresh
parts. But this is not all. On the eve of a new
production actors are frequently required to re-
hearse all day, act at night, then, after the per-
formance, to rehearse till four or five in the
morning, rehearse again from ten till four, and
then appear in the new production at night,
when, if there is, as might be expected, a want of
life discernible in the performance, the manager
is most likely in a furious temper because they do
not “ play up.” And in nearly every case this
continual strain is the outcome not of any actual
necessity, but of bad and ignorant stage manage-
ment. And what are the wages for this laborious
work? A little higher than obtain in the English
provinces, where living is cheap, and where the
hours of actual work instead of being from ten to
fourteen per day are seldom, owing to the long
runs, more than four or five. Excluding operatic
artistes, those who are, at the present time, re-
ceiving more than ten pounds per week can be
very easily counted, while for the rank and file of
the profession, five pounds would probably be too
high an average. In case of illness, even when
caused by overwork or the insanitary state of
the theatre even their paltry allowance is at
once stopped, thus throwing the actor more and
more in the power of the manager.
Were it not for the lavish, often too lavish, ap-
plause with which an Australian audience always
greets those who work for their amusement, there
would be little compensation in the life of a
salaried actor out here. Overworked and under-
paid they have likewise to endure much in their
life behind the scenes, for, with one exception,
there is not a theatre in Australia which provides
dressing-rooms for all the artistes of ordinary
comfort and decency.
The fact that one well-known manager is in
the habit of facetiously alluding to the
members of his company as his “slaves,” is
a fair example of the consideration shown
to the ordinary artiste, and a little anecdote
which also has the advantage of being true
will still further illustrate this. A fairly well-
known actor, temporarily out of harness, called at
the stage-door of a certain Australian theatre for
the purpose of soliciting an engagement. He was
met by Cerberus. “ What do you want?” “To
see Mr. A. or Mr. B.” “What’s yer name?”
“Smith.” “A hactor?” “Yes.’ “Want an
engagement?” “Yes.” “ Come along ’ere,” and
leading the way to the office he introduced the
visitor with the remark, “Smith; ’ere's Mr. A.
and ’ere’s Mr. 8., be’ave yerself.” He had taken
the cue from his masters. And yet most of these
men have in a few years, mainly by the exertions
of their paid artistes, risen from nothing.
With real and solid grievances to redress is an
actors’ union impossible ? It would almost seem
so. Of advantage only to the salaried artiste,
none of these dare take any part in its inception,
for to do so would mean forfeiting any prospect of
obtaining an engagement in the Australia. The
only chance would be for some one outside the
profession, and careless of managerial wrath, to
take the initiatory steps, for, once formed, the
advantages would be too patent for it not to be
eagerly taken advantage of by the profession
generally, and no manager would be strong enough
to resist its influence. Blackleg labour would be
out of the question here. The crying needs of
the professi n are : A fairer rate of pay; the
revision of the present one-sided form of agree-
ment and the abolition of that pernicious c'ause
which gives a man a er a lien on an actor’s services
for one month in the, year without salary ; the
abolition of needlessly long and unnecessary
night rehearsals, nearly always the result of incom-
petent stage management; the establishment of
a benevolent fund or cases of sickness and real
distress (the present theatrical fund, being entirely
under the control of the managers, is of little use
to the profession generally); and, finally, the
compulsory erection of decent and suitable
dressing-rooms in all theatres.
And all these tilings are well within actors’grasp
provided the necessary union could be brought
about. Some artistes who read this may, perhaps,
be induced to consider whether the idea is impos-
sible, after all; but few will be found to dispute
the substantial accuracy of all the assertions
herein contained.* —Yours,
AN OLD HAND.
The Socialist in London.
Ah me ! how hot and weary here in town
The days crawl by !
How otherwise they go my heart records,
Where the marsh meadows lie
And white sheep crop the grass, and seagulls sail
Between the lovely earth and lovely sky.
Here the sun grins along the dusty street
Beneath pale skies:
Hark ! spiritless, sad tramp of toiling feet,
Hoarse hawkers’ curses, cries—
Through these I hear the song that the sea sings
To the far meadowlands of Paradise.
0, golden-lichened church and red-roofed barn—
O, long sweet days—
O, changing unchanged skies, strait dykes all gay
With sedge and water-mace—
O .fair marsh land desirable and dear—
How far from you lie my life’s weary ways !
Yet in my darkest night there shines a star
More fair than day ;
There is a flower that blossoms sweet and white
In the sad city.
That flower blooms not where the wide marshes gleam ;
That star shines only when the world is grey.
For here fair peace and passionate pleasure wave
Before the light
Of radiant dreams that make our lives worth life,
And turn to noon our night;
We fight for freedom and the souls of men—
Here, and not there, is fought and won our fight!
E. NESBITT.
Professor Sellar, whose death was cabled
the other day, was one of the two surviving sons
of Se’lar, the factor of the Sutherland estates, who
was the main instrument of the iniquitous High-
land Clearances of two generations ago, when
hundreds of families were evicted from their
ancestral holdings (held on clan tenures from the
remotest times) —one of the vilest acts of oppres-
sion ever exercised by even British landlords. The
other son, and now sole survivor, is Robert Sellar,
of Melbourne, Sir James M‘Culloch’s partner, and
one of Victoria’s wealthiest, as he is one of her
most miassu i ing, men. Mr. Robert Sellar is
credited with manifesting an ample and unpre-
tentious generosity, which is said by those who
know him best to be directed towards numer-
ous persons who persist in regarding his
beneficence as suggested by a desire to
atone for some of his progenitor’s doings.
In Campei;dovvn (Vic.) district, there are still
living several patriarchs who have the vividest
recollection of the Sutherland horrors, and by a
curious coincidence, Professor Sellar held for some
time interests and squatting property in the same
district. One of the professor’s sons, a youth of
great scholastic promise, died at St, Kilda, Mel-
bourne, a few years ago, from consumption, and a
sad sequel to the event was the death, a year or
two later, of Rev. Adam Moffatt, his tutor, and a
favourite pupil of the late Professor’s. Young
Moffatt, who was pronounced by Bishop Moor-
house “a spiritual presence in the flesh,” died
from the same disease and was buried in the same
grave.
Old Judge Molesworfch was always clear,
headed. In criminal cases he kept a watchful ev
on the interests of the accused, whether defended
or not. He never bullied juries, and yet had
way of showing where they were wrong. ,q Q *
years ago he tried a Pitzroy volunteer who had
deliberately raised his rifle and shot his wife dead
There was no excuse. Molesworth charged hor
for murder or nothing. The jury found in.,®
slaughter. The Judge, blinking at the prisoner
in that queer way he had, said ; “ Prisoner at tii
bar, the'jury have found you guilty of man 6
slaughter. As their belief must have been th if
you raised the gun to your shoulder by accident
put your finger on the trigger and took deliberate
aim by accident, and fired the weapon entirely bv
accident, I sentence you to 24 hours’ imprison-
ment.” The most astonished man in the crowd
was the said volunteer. u
From a Northern paper :—
Mrs. Conroy, the sufferer by the East Darr tiwedv nf
three years ago, is now hopelessly blind. Mr. Coimw
was on East Darr Station, and during his temporarv
absence his wife, for company, went to stay the nightat
the house of a married couple named Suttor. About n
o’clock she awoke with a pain in her arm as from a bite
but it was not at first very severe. The stillness of her
I2-months’-old baby girl attracted her attention and tn
her horror, on examining the infant, she found it
just dying. The eyes were open and staring, and the
mouth also, but they closed just as death took place and
then the child looked as if in a deep sleep Five horn
afterwards the body turned quite black. There being no
medical man within a great distance, the cause of death
remained a mystery until, six nights afterwards, a three
year-old child of the married couple in whose home Mrs
Conroy’s infant died, was found dead at the same hour of
night, and in the same bed. Mr. Conroy and his wife
were summoned, and the bereaved parents stood aghas
at the second mysterious death under exactly similar cir- i
cumstances. Their uncertainty was, however, soon dis-
pelled, for Mr. Conroy, happening to turn down the
coverlet of the bed, found and killed a snake 17in. long,
light brown in colour with a black head marked with
minute white spots. The body of the dead child was
examined, and on one of the hands were found four little
punctures thus forming a square about three-
eighths of an inch. 'In five hours the body of
Suttor’s child turned clack, conclusively proving that
both the victims had met with the same end. Mrs.
Conroy’s arm was much swollen for several days after the
night she slept at Suttor’s house, and she suffered great
pain, but gradually the swelling diminished, and the arm
recovered its normal health. Four little marks exactly
similar to those seen on the hand of Suttor’s child were
found on Mrs. Conroy’s arm, and still remain there.
Within a month after the death of her child Mrs. Conroy’s
\\sion became partially obscured, and very soon she
became quite blind. For over two years her case was
under the care of doctors out West, Mr. Conroy’s means
not al\wing him to send her south. Four months ago,
however, the shearers and rouseabouts on Lerida,
Katandra, and Rockwood subscribed £130 towards the ex-
penses of a trip to Sydney to consult the specialists there,
but their kindness has only served to demonstrate the
hopelessness of her case. It is thought the snake—the
nights being cold—crept into the tent and thence to the
bed for warmth ; that it was disturbed, bit the child first,
and then the mother, and that, having expended its
venom in the child, it had not the same deadly poweratits
second effort. The four punctures, Mr. Conroy.says, con-
clusively prove that (as lately proved by Mr. Bray, of
Sydney) the assertion of naturalists, that snakes having
more than two fangs are not poisonous, is incorrect. Mrs.
Conroy is only 26 years of age, and of robust physique
The doctors attribute her blindness to nervous shock.
